HARBOUR AREA AND LAGOON WAVE CLIMATE
The waves entering the Klaipeda Strait and harbour areas from the sea gradually die out.
However, from time to time oscillations of the water level occur in the harbour area. This
phenomenon is rather dangerous for ships staying near quays. Harbour oscillations can
reach 50 cm in height, with 30 - 300 second periods.
During severe storms, 3 - 4 meter high waves were observed in the northern part of the
Klaipeda Strait, whereas 1 meter high waves occurred in the central part of the harbour.
Wave observations in the lagoon are performed at Nida (since 1948), Juodkrante (since
1954) and Vente (since 1954). Low but steep waves are induced by strong winds in the
shallow lagoon. The highest waves recorded reached 2.2 m in the central part of the lagoon
(1959 - 1961). In most cases (60%) wave height is about 0.5 m.
4.3.3  Coastal sediment transport
HISTORIC DATA
The present contours of the entire Lithuanian coastline of the Baltic Sea were formed about
6000 years ago after the maximum of the Lithuanian Sea transgression. In the past the outlet
of Klaipeda Strait was not fixed by breakwaters. Historic data show that in the period 1746-
1796 the Curonian spit became 565 m longer and another 75 m in the period 1796-1820 (i.e.
8.5 m/yr.). At the same time the northern coast of the outlet retreated (180 m in the period
1796-1820 or 7.2 m/yr). Hence, the outlet migrated slowly northward.
In the period 1834-1878 the outlet was fixed by the construction of breakwaters. In the period
1834-1845 the first part of the northern breakwater was built. Then, in the period 1845-1878
the southern breakwater was built and the northern one extended because the northern
beach had accreted to the tip of the northern breakwater in 1845.
In 1957 the southern breakwater was extended by 350 m to its present length. Possibly the
northern breakwater was reduced in length by 60 m at that time.
In 1960-1962 the depth of the access channel was dredged to a depth of 10 -10.5 m below
Datum in the outer part of the channel and to 9 - 9.5 m in the inner part. In 1982-1983 the
depth of the outer part of the channel was further increased to the present depth of 12 -12.5
m. The width of the present channel is 100 m.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   4-11                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                        a
HARRIS
Figure 4-5 shows the response of the coastline on the human interference in the period 1835-
1990. Unfortunately, only the coastline in the immediate vicinity of the breakwaters is shown,
which makes it hard to see whether the coastal changes are only local or are part of large-
scale changes. Experts of Delft Hydraulics reported a mildly sloping beach profile south of the
outlet and a steep sloping profile north of it. They also observed demolished bunkers at
several hundreds of metres north of the northern breakwater indicating erosion.

Figure 4-5    Coastline development from 1835-1990 near Klaipeda Strait (according to

PRESENT OPINIONS ABOUT COASTAL SEDIMENT TRANSPORT
Available information indicates no unanimous opinion about the direction and quantity of the
littoral drift along the sand spit.
Local investigators suggest a north going sand drift of about 400,000 m3/yr. Partly this will be
based on littoral drift computations and partly on coastal behaviour near structures on the
coast.
Halcrow (1993) assumed a net southward sand drift, whereas experts of Delft Hydraulics
concluded during a site visit in September/October 1994 that a north going transport was
most likely, which was supported by littoral drift computations later on (1998). In their study
they concluded that the coastal behaviour at the outlet suggested that a southward littoral drift
took place in a period in the previous century, followed by a small north going transport later
on, at least after 1957. The small net longshore sand transport results from two opposing
gross contributions (i.e. in southern and northern direction), which can easily reach figures of
several hundreds of thousands of cubic meters per year (in each direction).
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    4-12                   September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                     H
HARRIS
In the present situation this means that the two coastal segments north and south of the
breakwater are more or less independent coastal systems. This means that there is hardly
any sand transport from the one segment to the other. For the south of the entrance this
means that the shoreline is rather stable. Due to the relatively large distance between the
waterline and the tip of the southern breakwater no significant quantities of sand are
deposited in the entrance channel. Or in other words: the sand remains in the coastal system,
the result being a stable coast.
North of the entrance, however, sediment that is transported in southward direction will be
deposited in the entrance channel. (The distance between the shoreline and the tip of the
northern breakwater is much smaller, due to the accretion that occurred from 1835 onwards.)
Quite relevant information for understanding the coastal behaviour are dredging data. Since
the outer channel is deepened by dredging, maintenance dredging was required to maintain
the design depth and width of the channel. It is mentioned that the annual maintenance
dredging did not exceed a volume of 100,000 m3 when the access channel was maintained at
a depth of 10 m. In the period after 1985 the maintenance of the channel (to a depth of 11.4
m) amounted to 150,000 - 200,000 m3/yr.
The dredged sediments were initially dumped close to the shore north of the outlet. Presently,
most of the spoil is dumped far from the shore (See also Section 4.7.2 Dredging and
disposal). A small part of the spoil consisting of clean sand from the marine sector of the
access channel is dumped closer to the shore north of the outlet in a water depth of about 20
m. At present the dredged material is deposited at a location from where it cannot return to
the coastal system. This means that the coastal system north of the entrance suffers from a
negative sand balance, which leads to the observed erosion. So, based on the above
analysis it seems that the observed erosion north of the entrance is not caused by a net
northward sediment transport, but by the fact that sand is regularly taken out of the coastal
system.
The largest shoreline retreat occurs at a few hundred metres north of the northern breakwater
(near the bunkers). This can be explained by considering the diffraction of waves from west
to southwestern directions.
4.3.4  Sediment transport Curonian lagoon - Baltic Sea
The lagoon area is a transitory basin for the fluvial and marine suspended matter. Depending
on hydrodynamics, bottom characteristics and particle size, part of suspended material is
accumulated within the lagoon, whereas the rest is transported to the sea.
The Nemunas river runoff is the most important source, which accounts for approximately
87% of the total amount of allochtonous material. The mean sedimentation ratio in the lagoon
is 3.2 mm per year.
Total annual seawater inflow volume is equal to the total lagoon volume, however the amount
of material transported by inflows accounts for only 1.6% of total sedimentary lagoon material.
Otherwise, nearly half of the total suspended matter entering the lagoon is transported to the
sea.
Sedimentary matter fluxes in the Klaipeda Strait have been evaluated on the basis of field
investigations (filtration of suspensions and sediment traps).
Concentration of suspensions in the Klaipeda Strait ranges between 3.5 and 58.8 mg/l. Near
the bottom sediment concentrations are higher than in the surface layer. Judging by the data
obtained from sediment trap studies very intensive sediment transport takes place in the
western passage of Curonian lagoon, and the port fairway. The intensity of sediment fluxes in
the close vicinities of harbour embankments and semi-enclosed bays is rather low. The most
J
H-7371   Drall Final Environmental Impact Assessment   4-t3                 September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                        9
HARRIS
intensive fluxes of sedimentary matter develop in the port gate aquatory (Figure 4-6).
Sediment transport is seasonally changeable and the most changes occur in autumn and,
sometimes, spring.
SEDIMENTATION IN HARBOUR AREAS
The accumulation of sedimentary matter in the Klaipeda Strait is closely related to the
frequency of changes in hydrological situation, resulting in alterations of water flow directions.
During most of the year seaward sediment fluxes prevail, but there are periods when water
flows are directed from the sea into the lagoon. Under the conditions of abrupt decrease in
water flow velocity, mass accumulation of transported material in local bottom areas may
occur. This phenomenon takes place in all seasons. Most intensive filling up of the port
aquatory takes place under the conditions of winds when marine sediments are transported
into the strait. Sometimes the thickness of the sediment layer exceeds 1 m in certain parts of
the aquatories.

4.4  GEOLOGY, TOPOGRAPHY AND SEDIMENT CHARACTERISTICS
4.4.1   Geological history
HISTORIC DEPOSITS
Geological information shows that beneath recent sediments two types of deposits can be
found in the study area. These deposits were formed during the stages of the Baltic Sea
evolution, being mainly the Pleistocene and later on the Litorina stage of the Holocene.
The Pleistocene originated sediments are known as "glacial till" and consist of grey, dense
tills (loams, sandy loams), containing gravel, pebble and boulders. The glacial till layer
reaches a thickness of 40-60 m. The top of glacial deposits ranges from 9.2 - 15.8 m and
dips from the east to the west towards the Curonian spit. On top of the till, the younger glacial
deposits of the Litorina period are present. These sediments consist of sand enriched with
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    4-14                   September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project
HARRIS
organic material and peat sublayers. These sand layers are 5 - 8 meters thick, but in some
local areas where excavations have been carried out, outcrops of clayey and sandy loams
(tills) occur.
RECENT DEPOSITS
The recent sediments in the Klalpeda Strait consist of lagoon and sea originated material.
The composition of recent sediments in the Klaipeda straits is not uniform, due to differences
in sedimentation and erosion processes and dredging activities.
In the central part of the Strait, where sediment transport occurs, the recent sediment layer
thickness is less than 20-30 cm. In the dredged parts of the Strait the accumulated silty
sediment layer thickness reaches 0.4 - 1.2 m. The highest variation in sediment layer
thickness observed, is in the port gates area and in the access channel where, after storm
events, up to 1 - 1.2 m thick sand layers can be formed.
4.4.2  Bed topography
The Curonian lagoon has a mean depth of 3.8 meters. In the southern region of the lagoon
the maximum depth reaches 5.8 meters.
The port area is dominated by depths of 7-12 m. The northern part and sea channel are the
deepest parts in the harbour. The sea channel depth varies in range from 12 - 13.2 m, while
the depths near the Oil terminal and KLASCO embankments reach 12 - 12.5 m. The
navigational channel to the "Bega" embankment also reaches 12-12.5 m. From this "Bega"
embankment southward the navigational route becomes shallower from 9.7 - 10.4 m to 8.2 -
8.7 meters. In the southern part of the port depths in the International Ferry manoeuvre circle
reach 8.8 - 10 meters, while in the mooring locations it even reaches 11 -12 meters. At Malku
bay depths of 9 - 10 meters are present.
At present the bathymetry of the port area is still subject to change. In 1998, deepening of the
sea channel and northern part of the port to -14 meters was started. At a later stage, other
parts of the port area, including the embankments from the port gates to the mouth of Dane
River and its embankments will be deepened to -14 meters as well. The "Bega" and
"Klaipedos Smelte" embankments will be deepened to -12 m, while the rest of the harbour
area will reach 10 m depth.
The sandy bottom of the coastal zone gradually slopes from zero at the beach to a depth of
50 meters some 30 kilometres offshore. Two kilometres offshore Klaipeda water depth is
approximately 15 meters.
4.4.3  Sediment characteristics
TYPES OF SEDIMENT
Sediment data received from the Institute of Geography and the Lithuanian Energy Institute
(1998) show that the foreshore of the beaches on both sides of the outlet consists of fine and
medium sand above the 10 m depth-contour (Figure 4-7). Medium sand is found above the 5
m depth-contour north of the outlet and only above a level close to Chart Datum south of the
outlet. Below the 10 m depth-contour and around the unprotected part of the access channel
at sea the sediments are finer, mostly sandy coarse silt.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   4-15                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                                                        5
HARRIS
Inside the Klaipeda Strait, in the deep part of the harbour area the bottom mostly consists of
fine sand, silty sand and mud (Figure 4-8).
The dredged spoil of the harbour area consists of mud mostly originating from the lagoon and
fine sand originating from the coast.
The bottom  of the lagoon generally will consist of muddy deposits due to the absence  of
strong flow velocities and wave action.

The thickness of the soft surface sediments varies from a few centimetres inside the port
area to several meters in the access channel. Below the surface sediments a layer of glacial
till or "boulder clay" can be found. The several meters thick layer of boulder clay is very hard
(close to rock) with enclosures of stones, cobbles and boulders in varying degree of
incidence.
GRAIN-SIZE DISTRIBUTION
Grain-size composition of bottom sediments is given in Table 4-9. In the most dynamic areas
of the strait the peculiarities of grain-size distribution of bottom sediments have a seasonal
pattern. A periodical replacement of bottom sediments takes place in the fairway and western
passage of the Curonian lagoon where the types and grain-size composition of sediments
reflect the seasonal changes of current speeds. Under the maximal current velocities bottom
erosion occurs, whereas, when the current velocities slow down the silt material starts to
deposit. The most stable composition of bottom sediments is observed in the aquatories of
the central and southern embankments of the port and in closed bays where the
accumulation of thin dispersed matter takes place and silty-clayey mud develops.
H7371      Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment       4-16                                        September 1999

NORMS AND CLASSIFICATION SYSTEM FOR CONTAMINATED SEDIMENTS
The bottom sediments of the Klaipeda Strait are subdivided into four classes:
1. Non-contaminated sediments, i.e. sediments in which concentrations of chemical
elements do not exceed the background values, according to the separate lithological
types of the bottom sediments in the Curonian lagoon and the Baltic Sea.
2. Slightly contaminated sediments, i.e. sediments in which concentrations of chemical
elements exceed the background values, but do not exceed values, characteristic for
non-contaminated sediments (listed in the London Dumping Convention and other
classifications). Dumping of these sediments into the sea does not deteriorate the quality
of seawater and does not influence sea organisms toxicity.
3. Contaminated sediments, which may affect sea organisms but, nevertheless, their
dumping into the sea is permitted.
4. Highly contaminated sediments, which are potentially dangerous to sea organisms.
The limiting concentration values for the third and fourth classes were determined according
to the London Dumping Convention and data of other soil classification systems, as well as
estimated parameters of detected levels of contamination of the bottom sediments in
separate aquatories. Dredged sediments falling in the classes 1 and 2 may be dumped in the
sea or used for other purposes. Sediments falling in the classes 3 and 4 may only be dumped
in the sea in exceptional cases.
This classification system for bottom sediments is presented in the 'Rules of dredging and
cleaning of the Klaipeda port aquatory and sea channel and dumping of soils in the sea". The
class criteria are summarised in Table 4-10. The complete text of this document is included
as Appendix B to this report.
Table 4-10
Sediment classification system for dredging and disposal purposes in the Klaipeda region.

The 1999 sediment investigations campaign in the outer part of the Klaipeda port area
as part of the Worldbank EIA and dredging study
As part of the Environmental Impact Assessment for the "Kiaipeda Port Entrance
Rehabilitation Project', a sediment quality assessment was been carried out in the outer part
of the Klaipeda port area. This is the area relevant for the dredging study forming part of this
Woridbank project. The aim  of this assessment was to assess the thickness and the
characteristics of the sediment layers present, including the nature, the concentration and
distribution of pollutants. Sampling consisted of both surface sampling and borings. Surface
sampling was carried out by a diver scooping a sample with a bucket. The borings for core
sampling were done with a diver operated pneumatic hammercorer with stationary piston.
The sampling results in the form of core descriptions are included as Appendix E
Sampling and sampling locations
In total 22 sediment cores were taken in the project area. The sampling locations are
indicated in Figure 4-9.

Bioavailability testing
Besides physical and chemical testing bioassay testing (bioavailability of pollutants) was
carried out on 9 surface samples (marked A to K in the above figure). Testing was not
possible on samples C (bottom completely covered with small and big boulders) and H
(solid till).
Whole sediment toxicity of the nine samples was assessed through 10-day exposures with
the amphipod, E. estuarius. The organisms, E. estuarius used in testing were 3-5 mm in
size. After ten days, E. estuarius exposed to the nine samples of sediment from Klaipeda,
did not show a statistically significant reduction in survival when compared to the survival of
organisms in the control. In accordance with the 1991 USEPA guidance for dredged
sediment testing projects, samples are considered toxic if survival of organisms exposed to
test sediment are at least 20% less than the survival observed in the reference sediment
and this difference is statistically significant after a ten-day test period. The results indicate
H7371     Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   4-20                                     September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                            t
HARRIS
that sample I (in front of the oil terminal), is statistically significant when compared to the
control however, since survival in the sample was within 20% of the control it is not
considered toxic under USEPA guidelines. No other samples were statistically significant.
The testing results are summarised in Table 4-12. The full testing report is included as
Appendix G.

The 1995 Haskoning report "Study on the dredging and disposal of contaminated
sediments, Klaipeda port, Lithuania".
In 1995, HASKONING (Royal Dutch Consulting Engineers and Architects) executed a "Study
on the dredging and disposal of contaminated sediments, Klaipeda Port, Lithuania". Volume 1
(Inventory of existing data, by courtesy of HASKONING) included some data on levels of
sediment contamination in the port.
Essentially th'le report iocuses on the contamination and cleaninn of Malku bay. However, in
Annex 19 of Volume 1 "sampling locations, analyses results and soil stability properties of
sediments in other parts of the Klaipeda port" are presented. The inventory is based on 175
samples in the entire port, not specifying in further detail the exact locations of these
samples. In Table 4-15 average concentrations of heavy metals and hydrocarbons are
presented (up to the Dane River).

Summary on sediment quality
As has been demonstrated at many other contaminated sediment sites world-wide, the
results of these investigations showed a direct relationship between the percentage of fine
particles (< 0.063 mm) and the level of pollution. The more fine particles the higher the level
of pollution. Consequently the types of sediment (based on composition and grain size)
present in the study area can be linked to a level of contamination and zones with different
levels of contamination can be assessed. Following the classification of the bottom sediment
contamination as presented in Table 4-10, areas with class 1, 2 and 3 type contaminated
sediments were identified in the study area.
The surface sediments in the approaches to the Klaipeda port entrance up to oil terminal 2,
which is the World Bank dredging study area (seawards from gridline 27), comprise only
clean or slightly contaminated sediments. No restriction is required in relation to disposal or
beneficial use of these sediments from a contamination point of view.
The remaining surface sediments falling under this Worldbank EIA, present in the area
stretching from oil terminal 2 up to the Dane river comprise class 3 contaminated sediments.
The class 3 type sediments can be found practically all over the area. As sampling has been
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   4-24                September 1999



Kiaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                      a
HARRIS
limited to the top 5 cm surface layer of the bottom, quantification of the total volume of
contaminated sediment is not possible. According Lithuanian regulations these sediments
can only be dumped at sea in exceptional cases.
In other parts of the port (outside the scope of this EIA) several locations can be found
containing class 3 or class 4 contaminated sediments.
Based on the geophysical and bathymetrical investigations as carried out by the Research
Institute "Geoprojektas", and the - 14 m design level, it is clear that in a part of the dredging
area also hard glacial till is to be dredged. The glacial till is of historic origin and is not
contaminated.
4.5    WATER CHARACTERISTICS OF THE BALTIC SEA, KLAIPEDA STRAIT,
CURONIAN LAGOON
The Curonian Lagoon is characterised by the intensive water exchange with the Baltic Sea.
The water is constantly moving either towards the sea (80 % of cases) or to the lagoon.
The hydro-chemical regime of the lagoon and the Klaipeda Strait is affected by several
factors: river and sea inflow, municipal wastewater and shipping. The hydro-chemical regime
of the coastal waters is affected by the outflow of fresh water from the lagoon.
4.5.1   Water temperature
Water temperature fluctuations in the northern part of the Curonian lagoon depend on air
masses advection, solar radiation and river inflows. The temperature regime in the well-mixed
lagoon shows no significant variation between open and littoral parts. Some vertical gradients
form only during short periods of calm weather. The temperature of the coastal Baltic Sea
waters also depends on solar radiation, air masses advection and lateral water transport.
During warm seasons the upper water layer is nearly homogeneous due to wind action. The
exception is when east and north winds blow the warmed coastal waters into the open sea
causing cold deep-water upwelling. The deep-sea layers have nearly constant temperature.
During winter the temperature of the enclosed lagoon is nearly uniform around 0 0C, and only
in the harbour area the temperature is higher (0.1-0.4 C) due to warmer seawater advection.
The warming of the lagoon normally starts in April when the ice cover melts and air
temperature reaches 8.0-10.0 0C. The highest temperature in lagoon is recorded in the mid-
summer period when water temperatures reach 16.0-21.0 C, water temperature in the
Klaipeda strait will be 2.0-3.0 OC colder, due to seawater inflow. In the Baltic Sea maximum
upper layer temperature reaches 18.0-20.0 OC in August. Winter and spring temperature
depends on winter yearly variations reaching 5.0-7.0 0C. During springtime the coastal waters
in the vicinity of the Port entrance are affected by the outflow of warmer waters from the
lagoon (10.0-11.0 0C). In autumn seawater is well mixed due to storm events.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   4-25                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                            
HARRIS
4.5.2   Oxygen levels
Dissolved oxygen concentrations in the Curonian lagoon vary depending on the time of the
year.
In spring, when the temperature is still low and photosynthetic activity is high, the water is
saturated up to 170% of oxygen in the central part of the lagoon and even in the polluted
Malku bay oxygen levels still reach 78% saturation.
During summer time oxygen saturation decreases, but critical concentrations were observed
only in the harbour area. In the Malku bay bottom layer it drops to 3.7 mg/I, which equals 41%
of saturation. Some oxygen depletion also occurs in the harbour area near the main city
sewer. The situation during autumn is more or less similar showing lowest oxygen values
near the sewer and Malku bay. During wintertime no oxygen depletion was observed.
In the Baltic Sea the maximum oxygen concentrations in the photosynthetically productive
layer in the open sea reach 16 mg/I while in the nearshore area they reach 13-14 mg/l. This
means that the oxygen saturation levels are 113-115 %.
4.5.3   Background turbidity levels
Turbidity is used as a measure of the "clarity" or "cloudiness" of a liquid. It is defined as a
reduction of transparency caused by the presence of undissolved matter. It gives a good
indication of the appearance of a water in an aesthetic sense, but it is also an indicator of the
amount of suspended material. It is essentially an optical parameter and can be measured in
a number of ways, ranging from the relatively simple Secchi disk to electronic turbidimeters.
Suspended matter or suspended sediment is related to the weight of the solids in water and
as such it is not the same as turbidity. However, the two are interrelated and for a specific
site a relationship between the two can be established.
Background natural turbidity levels are determined by the development of plankton organisms
as well as by wind induced resuspension events, which are especially important in the shallow
Curonian lagoon areas. Sediment-rich river water inflow also influences turbidity levels. Both
in the lagoon and Klaipeda strait the turbidity levels show obvious seasonal variation (Table
4-16). Increasing wave heights in the lagoon will result in increased levels of resuspended
sediments and consequently reduced transparency. This relationship is presented in Table 4-
17.

Suspended sediment fluxes in the Klaipeda Strait have been repeatedly evaluated on the
basis of water samples (Pustelnikovas, 1983, Galkus, 1995). Suspended matter
concentrations in the Klaipeda Strait range between 3.5 and 58.8 mg/l (Table 4-18). Near the
bottom the concentrations are higher than in the surface layer.

A considerable decrease of suspended matter levels takes place in the nearshore zone near
the Klaipeda Strait mouth. This is the unloading area of sedimentary matter flows from the
Curonian lagoon. Previous investigations revealed that concentrations of suspensions in the
nearshore waters decrease to 2.4-21.8 mg/I (average 9.2 mg/I), while in the Klaipeda Strait
they reach levels of tens of mg/I. Approximately 85% of the suspended matter is composed of
particles larger than 0.01 mm in diameter. The largest part of it consists of detritus. Of the
suspended matter, sediment particles make up about 23% in the surface layer, to 40% in the
near-bottom layer.
In the access channel to the port, which can be seen as a continuation in the sea of the
Klaipeda Strait, the outflow of suspended matter can easily be traced to a depth of 20 -25 m.
The main mass of sedimentary matter migrates in the western and northwestern direction. To
the south from the southern jetty the concentration exceeding 5.0 mg/l in the near-bottom
layer occupies only a narrow strip of the nearshore till a depth of 5-13 m.
In the greatest part of the underwater shore slope the concentrations of suspended matter in
the near-bottom layer usually range from 3 - 5 mg/l. In only a few areas concentration values
below 3 mg/I stand out on this background. These areas almost coincide with the outcrops of
till deposits affected by the processes of recent bottom erosion. It is to be noted that the
given marine turbidity levels are related to measurements under calm weather conditions. No
data are available for suspended matter levels during stormy weather conditions.
4.5.4  Salinity
Salinity is a measure of the mass of dissolved salts. It reflects the relative proportions of
freshwater and seawater and is an important habitat descriptor. Salinity is usually measured
in "practical salinity units" (PSU), which are nearly identical to parts per thousand (3O/6 or ppt).
Salinity of the Atlantic Ocean is approximately 35%.. The Baltic Sea is more brackish, with
decreasing salinity from the west to the east and from the south to the north.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    4-27                   September 1999

HARRIS
Salinity levels in the coastal waters near Klaipeda depend on fresh water outflow from the
Curonian lagoon (Figure 4-11). The average summer salinity levels range between 5.0 and
7.0/., which could drop to 4.6/^ due to the above reason.

However, strong salt-water intrusions can also occur in summer, when a low water level in the
lagoon is observed. The salinity stratification in the lagoon (meaning that the surface layer is
occupied by the outflow of the lagoon's water, while the sea water is entering as a near
bottom layer) is unstable.
4.5.5   Pollution
Two types of pollution can be distinguished in the study area, being pollution caused by
nutrients and pollution caused by heavy metals and organic chemicals.
Most of the nutrients are transported to the lagoon through the 937 km (475 in Lithuania)
Nemunas River. This river carries municipal, industrial and agricultural pollution from the
1000458 km2 area of the Nemunas river basin. The annual nutrient dynamics in the lagoon
depend on its biological cycles. Increased nutrient concentrations are also recorded for the
area near the main Klaipeda sewer. As a consequence of the load of nutrients, both the
lagoon and harbour area are very eutrophicated.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    4-28                    September 1999
H7371    Draft Final Environmental lmDact Assessment    4-28                   September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project
HARRIS
Besides the load of heavy metal and organic chemical pollution brought to the area by the
upstream rivers, several local sources can be identified:
*  discharge of municipal and industrial waste water (city sewers, shipyards, oil industry,
ships)
*  rainwater runoff from industrial sites in the port
*  accidental spills
*  the contaminated sediments present in the Port area, which once formed start to act as a
source itself (especially in Malku Bay, but also at the Klasco commercial port, the
Klaipeda Ship repair yard, the bays of the second ferry link, the paper mill enterprise, the
rail ferries swinging place, Smelte port and the Dane river mouth).
The average oil hydrocarbons concentration in the lagoon reaches 0.03 mg/I. Bottom
sediments are also slightly polluted: in the Klaipeda Strait near 42.7 mg/kgws, near the Paper
Mill 41.2 mg/kgws. Oil hydrocarbon concentrations could increase rapidly during oil spills,
which is not uncommon in a harbour. Other organic chemicals detected in the waters and
sediments of the study area include chlororganic pesticides, detergents, and phenols.
Mercury (Hg) concentrations in the lagoon are rather low. Some higher Hg concentrations
were found in the bottom sediments in the Malku bay near the city sewer. During last years
Hg concentrations in the Baltic Sea has tendency to decrease. The average Hg concentration
in the seawater is 0.03 mg/I.
The Hg concentration in the bottom sediments also has the tendency to decrease with a
present average of 0.06 mg/kgws.
Other heavy metals like tin, lead, cadmium and copper were also found in the bottom
sediments. For more information on sediment contamination reference is made to Section
4.3 of this report.
The outflow from the Curonian lagoon also carries contaminants and nutrients to the Baltic
Sea. When entering the sea, this water penetrates up to 12 miles west and, due to the
dominating currents, up to 50 km north.
In the sea the existing disposal sites for dredged sediments can also be seen as a source for
sea water contamination, as not only clean sediments have been dumped at these areas. In
the Baltic Sea, the highest levels of hydrocarbon contamination in water and bottom samples
can be found near the dumping areas.
4.6    ECOLOGICAL CHARACTERISTICS
4.6.1   Baltic Sea marine ecology
The Klaipeda strait area and marine aquatory contain pure communities of soft bottom
invertebrates and are almost devoid of benthic vegetation. Only in the deeper areas (10 - 20
meters) on a stony bottom rather abundant filtration feeders (blue mussel (Mytilus edulis) and
barnacle (Balanus improvisus)) occur. Otherwise the region is of great importance as
migration route of spawning migration for salmon (Salmo salar), sea trout (Salmo trutta
trutta), smelt (Osmerus eperlanus), vimba (Vimba vimba) and other. Freshwater fishes from
the lagoon migrate to the neighbouring sea areas for feeding on benthic animals.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   4-29                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                      m
HARRIS
TYPES OF HABITAT AND KEY SPECIES
The sea area, neighbouring the outlet of the Curonian lagoon is characterised by permanent
influence of winds, waves and water currents, which produces a very hydrodynamically active
environment. Therefore there is no oxygen deficiency and no oxygen based gradients in the
distribution of bottom biota, as occurs in the offshore deeper areas. The main physical
factors, which determine the local scale distribution of benthic biotopes and bottom
communities include bottom substrate, waves effect, light conditions and desalinating effect
from the lagoon outflow. The main types of bottom biotopes in the area under consideration
are listed below:
* An exposed sandy beach (on sites with admixture of gravel and pebbles) swashed by
waves and flooded by water during storms, often with swells of decomposing macroalgae.
No macrofauna and flora is present except scarce specimens of the air breathing
amphipod Talorchestia deshayesii, but interstitial meiofauna occurs in the lower wet
sections.
* A narrow depression of 0.5-1.5 m depth made by bores along the sandy beach face in
the uppermost part of the submarine slope. In summer time it contains mats of floating,
partly decomposing macroalgae (mostly filamentous greens with admixture of red algae
Furcellaria lumbricalis), which are densely inhabited by amphipods Gammarus and
Bathyporeia.
* Sands, movable by waves and currents from 1 to 5 m depth. Inhabited by mobile
burrowing amphipods (Bathyporeia and Corophium species), shrimps (Crangon) and
mysid shrimps (Neomysis and Mysis), the tube dwelling polychaete Pygospio elegans and
infaunal "borrowers" (oligochaetes, polychaetes Nereis diversicolor and Marenzelleria
viridis) as well as fish fry. The bottom animal community consists of 1 to 4 species with
total abundance of 300 - 6000 ind./m2 and biomass of 0.6 - 4.3 g/m2.
* Stony bottoms at the depth from approx. 8-10 m to 20-25 m with dense aggregations of
stationary epifaunal suspension feeders, the blue mussel Mytilus edulis and the barnacle
Balanus improvisus, colonies of hydrozoan Cordylophora caspia and single specimens of
the red algae Ceramium rubrum (occurs down to 20 m). The bottom animal community
comprises up to 50 species in total and is characterised by very high biomass (up to
1747 g/m2) and abundance (48000 ind./m2). This community functions as a biofilter,
cleaning and lightening the water.
* Sand and coarse aleurite bottoms on sites with admixture of mud at the depth from 5 m
to 30 m (beneath the most intensive wave action zone) with the bivalve molluscs
Macoma balthica and Mya arenaria, polychaetes Pygospio elegans and Nereis
diversicolor, amphipod Corophium volutator. Usually the community consists of 8-10
bottom macrofauna species, its abundance as well as biomass vary in a wide range
(1000 - 7000 ind./m2 and 100 - 600 g/m2). This is the most widely distributed bottom
community throughout the area, serving the important feeding ground for benthofagous
fish of both marine and freshwater origin.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental impact Assessment   4-30                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                        9

Figure 4-12 Underwater biotops near Lithuanian Baltic coast
UNDERWATER HABITATS OF THE KLAIPEDA PORT BREAKWATERS
Underwater biological surveys undertaken in 1995-1997 (Figure 4-13) have shown that the
breakwaters' boulders, concrete blocks and tetrapods form plentiful artificial habitats for
benthic algae, invertebrates and fish. The stones are covered almost totally by dense
canopies of fucoid brown algae Pilayella sp. from the water surface down to 1.5 - 2 m depth
and by green algae Cladophora sp. in the depth range 2-4 m. Deeper, at the depth interval of
4-6 m the outer, exposed surfaces of stones are nearly free of fouling, here only sparse
specimens of the green algae and the barnacle B. improvisus occur. Colonies of the blue
mussel together with associated fauna are found at the inner, sheltered surfaces of stones.
Beneath 6 m the bottom is sandy with typical Macoma community. There is obvious
difference between communities inside and outside the breakwaters. The richest community
is outside the southern breakwater, not influenced by the lagoon outflow, while inside the
breakwaters epilithic communities are poor due to constant salinity and water quality
changes.

ZOOPLANKTON
The species composition, community structure and abundance of the zooplankton depend on
the water mass and the season: for instance, the crustacean zooplankton usually is most
abundant in July - August when the Curonian Lagoon waters prevail in Klaipeda Straight. In
Table 4-19 the zooplankton species are listed including the highest values of abundance in
the study area. Due to their abundance these organisms have a great possibility to become a
"hot spot" organisms in ballast waters.
Two of the indicated organisms, the polychaete Marenzelleria viridis and the zebra mussel
Dreissena polymorpha have shown a great invasive potential; during some periods their
plankton larvae are the dominant group of the zooplankton in the Klaipeda harbour area.

FISH SPECIES
The important commercially exploited fish species in the Baltic Sea in terms of abundance
are the demersal (live in, on , or near the bottom of the sea) species cod and flounder, the
pelagic (in water column) species herring and sprat, the anadromous (migrating from sea to
fresh water) species salmon and the catadromous (migrating from fresh water to the sea)
species eel. In addition, a number of other species, including several flatfish species, are also
exploited. In total some 30 different species of fish are caught in the Baltic Sea. Specific
information on fish species caught in the Lithuanian coastal zone is given in section 4.7.6
(fisheries) of this report.
SPAWNING AREAS
The whole study area is practically free of natural spawning grounds for commercial fish.
However, the breakwaters serve as artificial spawning grounds for the herring (Figure.4-14 a
and b) For instance in April 1995, the mean density of fish roe deposited on the concrete
blocks and tetrapods was 2 mln ind./m2; the maximum (14 min ind./m2) was found at the
depth of 0.8 m in the central part of the southern breakwater. The laboratory analysis have
shown that approx. 95 % of roes were healthy at least at the moment of sampling. The field
observations also showed that the northern breakwater is less suitable for spawning.
Before the spawning time (May - June) big numbers of turbot spent their time in the study
area. The precise numbers, however, are not available.

MIGRATION ROUTES AND TIMING
Both anadromic and catadromic migrations take place in the Klaipeda strait. In this respect
the areas near the end of the strait (5-7 km up to and including the breakwaters) are very
important. Direct information on the concrete time of migration across the strait for each
species is not available. All assumptions are based on fish catches data from the strait, the
sea and the lagoon.
In general, fish migrations across the strait take place throughout the year. The most
important migratory species, which have spawning migrations across Klaipeda strait, are:
H7371    Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    4-34                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                        9
HARRIS
1. Salmon (Salmo salar L.), Red Book, rare species, (the migration of reproducers - July -
December, smolts - April - June)
2. Sea Trout (Salmo trutta L.), Red Book, rare species, ( the migration of reproducers - July
- December, smolts - April - June),
3. Twaite shad (Alosa falax L.), Red Book, rare species (the migration of reproducers -
during the spring months ),
4. Sea lamprey (Petromyzon marinus L.), Red Book, rare species (no data available),
5. Sturgeon (Acipencer sturio L.), Red Book, rare species, may be disappeared species, (no
data available),
6. Whitefish (Coregonus lavaretus L.), rare commercial species (the migration of
reproducers -October - January),
7. Vimba (Vimba vimba L.) rare protected species (the migration of reproducers -October -
April),
8. Smelt (Osmerus eperlanus L.), migratory commercial species (the migration of
reproducers -October - March),
9. Eel (Anguilla anguilla L.), migratory commercial species(the migration of reproducers -
May - July),
10. Lamprey (Lampetra fluviatilis L.), migratory commercial species (the migration of
reproducers -October - April).
The feeding migrations of other fish species (bream, pikeperch, perch, flounder, herring,
three-spined stickleback and others) also take place. The timing of all migrations shows year-
to-year variations.
In the Coastal Zone seasonal, spawning or feeding migration take place too: spawning
migration of herring - April-June, turbot pre-spawning migration - April -June, feeding
migrations of juveniles of different species, etc.
POLLUTION
Evidently the sea areas around the port of Klaipeda are influenced by eutrophic lagoon water
and port wastes. However, it is extremely difficult to estimate the antropogenic part of waste
in the rather complicated (hidrologically and ecologically) system of the Klaipeda strait and the
Baltic Sea aquatory. In this respect it is to be noted that due to the many years that this
situation exists, most of the animal and plant species in the area are tolerant to polluted
waters, as well as to salinity, temperature changes and other unfavourable abiotic conditions.
4.6.2   Curonian lagoon ecology
Being at the end of several river basins and in open connection with the sea, the relatively
shallow Curonian lagoon functions as a sink for both fluvial and marine suspended matter.
Due to the large inflow of nutrients the lagoon is eutroficated and excessive algal blooms
have been recorded. Nevertheless, the lagoon still functions as an important aquatic habitat
with a wide variety of species, of which several are commercially important.
TYPES OF HABITAT AND KEY SPECIES
The habitats of the Curonian lagoon are characterised by low depths, active hydrodynamic
patterns and distribution of various sediment types. Soft and hard bottom habitats can be
distinguished for the benthic environment. The pelagic system is best characterised by the
salinity levels present, and can be divided into: limnic (freshwater), frontal and marine.
H7371    Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    4-35                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                      u
HARRIS
Key species are closely linked to the habitat dynamic and coupling between pelagic and
benthic systems. The first one responds to momentary changes (hours to days scale) of
environmental factors (mostly salinity and temperature), whereas the response of the second
system is restricted to the longer periods (weeks to months scale). Consequently, bottom
macrofauna permanently is a mixture of marine and freshwater species, however in the
pelagic system either fresh or limnic communities are dominant in most cases. Unstable
frontal pelagic communities are distributed in the narrow salinity range and undergo spatial
fluctuations.
The hard bottom habitat is the most diverse part of the benthic system. Key species are
epifaunal suspension feeders like: zebra mussel Dreissena polymorpha in the freshwater
conditions and barnacle Balanus improvisus in the brackish harbour strait. These habitats
include hard artificial and semi-natural substrates (piles, sunk wood, and embankment
boulders) to the boulders and other substrate of natural origin. Average weight and
abundance of macrofauna were estimated to be 51 g/m2 and 8x103 ind/M2 respectively.
Habitats with artificial substrates in many cases are periodically influenced by extreme
environmental factors (ice cover, wave breaking etc.), therefore macrofauna parameters
could be reduced in these areas. During the vegetation period, littoral hard bottom substrates
are covered by macroalgae communities of seasonal opportunistic filamentous green algae:
Cladophora, Enteromorpha, Chaetomorpha sp. and opportunistic red  algae  Bangia
atropurpurea.
Soft bottom habitats include coarse to fine grained sandy and muddy bottom types and are
inhabited by burrowing oligochaetes, chironomids, polychaetes Nereis diversicolor and
Marenzelleria viridis, etc. The average weight and abundance of the macrofauna communities
are estimated at approximately 17 g/m2 and 15x103 ind/M2 respectively. Seasonal
filamentous green algae mats are observed during the vegetation season only.
The distribution of the four main bottom macrofauna communities is presented in Figure 4-15
a and b.
Three types of pelagic habitats (limnic, marine and frontal) are forming different quantitative
and qualitative patterns of zooplankton community. The limnic habitat (up to 0.5 psu) is
characterised by comparatively high species diversity. There are 23 limnic crustacean
species 'and taxonomic groups found. The key species are Chydorus sphaericus, Daphnia
longispina, Cyclops strenuus and Mesocyclops leuckarti. At different stages of the seasonal
succession they reached more than 50 % of total zooplankton density ranging from 50 up to
550 ind/l. Most abundant representatives of Rotatoria are Keratella sp. and Brachionus sp.
In the marine zooplankton community 8 marine and brackish crustacean species were found
during Baltic Sea water intrusions. Monthly averages of total density were 10-15 times lower
than in limnic zooplankton community. Acartia bifilosa appeared as a dominant species with
the maximum density exceeding 25.0 ind/l.

In the junction of fresh and marine water masses the frontal community appeared at salinity
range 1.5-5.5 psu and is represented by a mixture of limnic and marine species. Slightly
higher species diversity and comparatively low density of limnic (50.0 - 180.0 ind/I) as well as
marine (2.0 - 19.0 ind/I) species are characteristic of frontal zooplankton community.
Phytoplankton community consists of 438 species, forms and varieties of plankton algae, 343
species of them were common both in the lagoon and in the adjacent Baltic Sea areas. Most
of the species belong to the fresh-to-brackish (37% of total species number) and fresh water
(42%) ecological groups, however the data on their distribution along the salinity gradient is
not available. In spring and summer-autumn periods key species are Stephanodiscus
hantzschii (Diatomophyceae) and Aphanizomenon flos-aquae (Cyanobacteria) with densities
up to 30 mg/l and 100 mg/l respectively.
Benthic macrofauna production is used as a food source for the demersal benthic fishes.
However, the grazing ratio of zooplankton within pelagic system is low. Nevertheless, not
grazed part of zooplankton communities in the water masses with critical salinity values are
the source of particulate organic matter. Due to high mortality, approximately 60-80% of the
limnic and 60-70% of marine community biomass at 0.5-2.0 and 5.0-6.5 psu respectively are
transported from pelagic to the benthic system.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    4-37                   September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project
HARRIS
FISH SPECIES
In the northern part of the lagoon the main commercially important species are: Smelt
(Osmerus eperlanus L.), pikeperch (Stizostedion lucioperca L.), bream (Abramis brama L.),
roach (Rutilus rutilus L.), eel (Anguilla anguilla L.), perch (Perca fluviatilis L.) and other illegally
fished rare protected migratory fishes.
The western coast of the lagoon is an important area for most migratory fishes: ide
(Leuciscus idus, asp (Aspius aspius), Vimba vimba, flounder (Piatichthys fiesus). The fish
communities there are formed of river species. Totally more than 30 fish species are known
to be common in the lagoon.
In the north part of Curonian lagoon local freshwater fish populations are reproducing.
POLLUTION
The Lithuanian monitoring programmes have documented fish communities clearly
influenced by eutrofication. In the Curonian lagoon, the abundance of cyprinids, such as
roach, silver bream and bream, is extremely high compared to most other coastal lagoon
areas.
On extreme accumulation sites and inside the harbour inlets the benthic macrofauna is
scarce or even absent, because of contaminated sediments and heavy organic pollution.
In the pelagic system approximately 4 % of the total number of phytoplankton species are
known to be potentially toxic. However only few species reach high abundance: the green
algae Scenedesmus quadricauda, the blue-greens Microcystis aeruginosa, Anabaena
spiroides and, especially A. flos-aquae. During intensive algae growth at temperatures around
20 0C, concentrations of A. flos-aquae several times reached "hyper-bloom" levels (higher
than 100 mg/I).
Both benthic and pelagic systems are of enhanced potential to be recipients of invasive
species. This phenomenon known as "biological pollution" may result in structural and
functional ecosystem changes. Totally 8 alien species are known to occur in the vicinity of the
harbour area and most of them belong to the different groups of benthic macrofauna. Two of
them, the polychaete Marenzelleria viridis and the zebra mussel Dreissena polymorpha
already dominate in the lagoon.
4.6.3  Curonian Spit National Park ecology
The Curonian spit is a patch of preserved land between the Baltic Sea and the Curonian
lagoon with a unique scenic beauty. Up to the 15th century both deciduous and coniferous
forests covered the spit. Later, however, large portions of them were felled by axe. This
resulted in severe sand shifting. The moving sand swallowed 14 villages. It was in 1825 when
reforestation projects were started to try to stop the moving sand. At present the spit is
covered by some 7 thousand hectares of forests, most of them pinewoods. On the old dunes
linden, oaks and elm trees can be found.

The mosaic type spreading of the vegetation in the Curonian Spit determines the distribution
of its fauna. Important mammals are: Moose or European Elk (Alces alces), Wild Pig (Sus
serofa), Sciurus vulgaris, Pine Marten (Martes martes), Roe Deer (Capreolus capreolus),
Brown Hare (Lepus europaeus), Fox (Leypes vulpes), Eurasian Badger (Meles meles),

4.7  SPECIAL FEATURES OF THE STUDY AREA
4.7.1   Navigation and vessel traffic control
TYPES AND NUMBERS OF SHIPS
Every year Klaipeda port is visited by more than 50 types and sizes of ships. These include
liquid, dry and neo bulk cargo ships, specialised cargo ships like Ro-Ro ferry's and container
ships, fishing ships and several non-merchant types of ships.
In 1997, more than 7000 ships (excluding small fishing ships and pleasure boats) called the
Klaipeda port. In some periods between 30-40 ships per day call on the port, resulting in
about 60-80 ships movements across the entrance of the port per day.
PILOTAGE
Pilotage in Klaipeda port is mandatory for medium and big sized ships, from the ports
'Receiving light buoy" at 2.5 nautical mile from port entrance, up to the entrance channel and
in all port areas. Exemptions of the mandatory pilotage are arranged for the smaller fishing
ships and pleasure boats, and for some of the Ro-Ro ferry ships. The latter, only if their
captains have a special permit from the harbour master. The Klaipeda port pilot station is
located at the harbour master office tower near quay wall No.4. Pilot's watch is kept 24 hours
a day except in periods of dense fog and strong SW - NW winds above force 7 of the
Beaufort scale.
Anchorage can be obtained in the outer roads at the receiving light buoy. The outer roads
anchorage for tankers is situated at a distance of 3.5 miles from this buoy where waterdepth
ranges from 31 - 35 m.
The pilot orders tugs if necessary. Mooring and unmooring of large sea-going vessels can be
done with tug assistance only. The number of tugs to be ordered depends on the weather
conditions and can be agreed upon between the captain and the pilot.
VESSEL TRAFFIC CONTROL ACTIVITIES
The port is equipped with a vessel traffic control system. The VTC-station is located at the
Harbour master office tower. Its radar stations are located on tower 1 (Harbour master office)
and on tower 2, which is located near the quay wall No. 72. Pilots-operators provide all vessel
traffic control from tower No. 1. All ships on the port entrance and in port are under vessel
traffic control. Radio contact with the vessel traffic control centre is established through the
Port Radio station, operating 24 hours per day.
H7371    Draft Final Environmental Irnpact Assessment   4-41                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                        N
HARRIS
ACCIDENTS IN KLAIPEDA PORT
The northern part of the Klaipeda port is not safe during strong western storms, because of
wave conditions. In these cases, ships located at quay walls No. 1-8 should sail to open sea
for the storm period, or must take additional safety actions, like tug assistance, or stay on
anchor in harbour. The situation for the ships is even more difficult during storms from west
or northwest directions near quay walls No. 1-4.
A few major accidents have occurred in Klaipeda port. The most serious accidents took place
in 1981 with the tanker GLOBE ASIMI and in 1987 with the cargo ship RUDOLF
BERSHTEIN. Wrecks of which now are near the head of the North breakwater. These
accidents occurred during strong westerly storms, when the ships tried to leave port, as it
was considered unsafe to stay near quay wall No. 3 (GLOBE ASIMI) and quay wall No. 7
(RUDOLF BERSHTEIN). During these stormy conditions with big waves at the entrance of
the port and varying current conditions in- and outside the port entrance, it is very difficult for
ships to leave or enter the port.
Smaller accidents involving small fishing boats took place between 1980-1992 at the entry of
the port and with a floating crane near port entrance, wrecks of which are on the North
breakwater. In 1987 and in 1988 minor accidents involving the Ro-Ro ferry ships took place
in the entrance to the port.
NAVIGATIONAL CONSTRAINTS DUE TO WAVES AND ICE FORMATION
As the port is not protected from westerly storms and related waves by natural banks or
breakwaters, incoming waves cause difficult navigational conditions. During these stormy
conditions it is difficult to both enter and leave the port. On entering the port, ships more or
less sail on the waves. Due to the fact that the ships have to slow down in speed, the
manoeuvring possibilities are strongly reduced. According to port regulations a ships speed
limitation is 6 knots. The accident with the Ro-Ro ferry ship MUKRAN on arrival to Klaipeda
port was attributed to insufficient manoeuvrability as a consequence of reduced speed.
As the existing entrance of the port is at rather short distance from the oil terminal quay walls,
where tankers with hazardous cargo (oil products) are mooring, this is an unsafe situation.
When ships depart the port during storm conditions, the same speed problem arises. Ships
must make a turn at the channel bending point at rather close distance from the port
entrance, where waves penetrate from the sea. During the turning process (about 25
degrees), a ships speed might drop from 6 knots to 3-4 knots, sometimes even to 1.5 knots,
like tanker GLOBE ASIMI. With these slow speeds the ships manoeuvrability is strongly
reduced as well and consequently the ships sailing line is dictated by waves, currents and
wind drift.
In general Klaipeda port is ice free in wintertime. Only in very cold winters (once per 30-40
years) the south part of the Baltic Sea and the port are covered by ice. In springtime and
some times in wintertime ice patches will shift from the Curonian lagoon to the sea via the
Klaipeda Strait. During very intensive ice shifting periods some risks exist for the ships
mooring near quay walls and for ships which can not use anchoring positions in port areas.
During ice shifting periods the small fishing ships and other medium sized ships face difficult
sailing conditions.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   4-42                  September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitabon Project                                          
HARRIS
4.7.2  Maintenance dredging and disposal
Klaipeda Port is the only area along the Lithuanian coast where maintenance and capital
dredging works are carried out. Some beach replenishment works north of Palanga may be
considered in the future, but these works do not fall within the scope of works of the Port
Authorities.
Maintenance dredging totals approximately 500,000 m3 a year and is mainly carried out in
three areas within the port:
*  The navigational channel outside the breakwaters, with quantities ranging from 70,000 to
500,000 a year. On an average dredged volumes totals approximately 200,000 m3 of
sandy sediments a year.
*  The harbour entrance, from the tip of the breakwaters up to and including the oil berths:
150,000 m3 of sand and mud.
*  The area in front of the international Ferry terminal: approximately 150,000 m3 of mud
and sand.
Since 1995 capital dredging works are being carried out in various parts of the port to
improve accessibility. These capital dredging works include dredging of the hard glacial till
sediments.
Klaipeda Port does not have its own dredging equipment. As there are also no Lithuanian
dredging contractors, foreign dredging companies are invited to bid for all dredging works. In
the past decade, Latvian, Russian and Polish dredging companies have carried out dredging
works.
Maintenance dredging (sand and silt) is mainly carried out with Trailing Suction Hopper
Dredgers. The capacity of the hoppers is 1200 to 3000 m3, while the average daily
productivity amounts to 3000-5500 m3 (small hopper) and 1.0000-11000 m3 (medium sized
hopper) of dredged material.
Capital dredging is mainly carried out with a bucket dredger in combination with a grab
dredger. The bucket dredgers used have a theoretical productivity of 750 m3 per hour.
However, the actual productivity varies, depending on the difficulty of dredging operations
and characteristics of the sediments encountered, in the range of 700 to 3500 m3 per day.
The relatively hard glacial till is difficult to dredge. According Russian classification this type of
material is classified as having a hardness of category 7. The next hardness category will
require blasting prior to dredging. Blasting however has not been allowed in Klaipeda since
1960.
The sediments from the glacial till layer contain varying quantities of boulders. These
boulders range in size from small stones to stones of more than 100 tons. Boulders which can
not be handled by the bucket dredger will be taken out by a grab dredger. If the boulders are
also too big to be removed by the grab dredger, sediments will be taken out from underneath
the boulder, causing it to sink to a lower position.
The boulders are collected (separated from the other sediments) and stored on the shore
near the southern breakwater. All other sediments, originating both from maintenance and
capital dredging are transported to offshore disposal sites where they are dumped.
A review of the volumes dredged since 1990 is presented in Table 4-20.

When the problem of disposing 300 thousand m3 of heavily contaminated sludge from Malko
Bay arose, the problem of intensive spreading of the sediments at the No. 3 disposal site
forced the discussion on other alternatives. As a result it was proposed to create an
additional disposal site (indicated No. 5 on the map) some 29 kilometres south-west of the
port gates, where the waterdepth is approximately 54 m. Sediment dispersion at this site is
expected to be directed towards deeper accumulation zones.
In 1996, a new disposal site has been approved for disposal of sand, which contains less
than 30% of particles smaller than 0.1 mm. This No. 4 site is located 11 kilometres NW from
port gate at depths of 25-30 m. At present only a small part of the clean sandy sediments
from the marine sector of the entrance channel are deposited here. This disposal site has an
experimental character and is not expected to have a significant effect on marine biological
resources.
DISPOSAL OF CONTAMINATED SEDIMENTS
In 1995 a study on "Dredging and disposal of contaminated sediments in Klaipeda Port' has
been carried out. The project identified and made preliminary designs for two possible sites
for storage of contaminated sediments, being the so-called Ship Graveyard and Dreverna.
The Ship Graveyard could accumulate polluted deposits from Malko Bay for short-term
storage, whereas at the Dreverna site a long-term storage site could be developed for
contaminated sediments from different parts of the entire port. The storage capacities for the
Ship Graveyard and Dreverna are 65 and 350 thousand M3.
However, the construction of these sites was considered to be too expensive and the
realisation of a confined disposal area for contaminated sediments did not materialise.
Consequently both clean and contaminated dredged sediments are disposed in the sea.
BENEFICIAL USE OF DREDGED SEDIMENTS
All dredged sediments from the Klaipeda port are disposed of in the sea and are not applied
for any practical use.
There are plans to carry out an introductory study on "Utilisation of dredged materials from
the Klaipeda Port Area", in 1998. This study should assess expediency of ground use and
formulate environmental requirements. This study is to include the utilisation of sand dredged
from the entrance channel and port gate areas.
4.7.3  Environmental protection and coastal zone management
REGULATIONS AND PROGRAMS
The rules and regulations in Lithuania with respect to environmental protection are described
in the Constitution of the Lithuanian Republic and the law on Environmental Protection.
The main environmental protection related limitations for the Klaipeda harbour development
in the study area are:
* the territories on the southern side of Klaipeda Strait belong to the Curonian Spit National
Park and the territories north of Giruliai belong to the Pajurio regional park. In these
locations no industrial, recreational or other activities can be started without permission of
Lithuanian Government. The same applies to the entire coastal zone starting 200 meters
from the water border.

* for the territory between Melnrage and Giruliai plans have already been proposed to
develop high quality infrastructure for the recreational and entertainment industry. (This

* the regulations for sustainable management and protection of fishery resources in the
study area.
At this time the "Project on Integrated Lithuanian coastal zone management (EU PHARE,
Project No: ZZ 92.18) is in the final stage. The main goal of this project was to ensure
sustainable use of coastal zone resources, protection of biodiversity and productivity of
coastal ecosystems and increase the quality of the coastal environment by integrated
planning and management activities.
For some environmental problems, the responsibility and timing is fixed in the Klaipeda
Municipality Environmental Protection Program.
Waste water treatment, drinking water quality and related management are handled by the
Klaipeda Environmental Protection Project, which is carried out as a joint project between
Lithuania, Sweden, Finland and the IBRD.
ANTI-POLLUTION AND MONITORING PROGRAMS IN THE PORT
At present there are no special anti-pollution programs in port area, but in 1998 the
Lithuanian Action Plan for the elimination of oil spills in the Baltic Sea has been prepared,
whereas some port companies have their own company oriented action plans for pollution
prevention.
Several environmental monitoring programs are being carried out in the study area. Co-
ordination between these programs is a point that requires attention.
The main monitoring program is "The environmental monitoring program of Lithuania".
Several parts of this program are also related to the present study area, like: the Klaipeda Air
Quality Observation Program, the Water Monitoring Program, which includes the Curonian
lagoon, the Baltic Sea and groundwater. This program was started at 1995. The Baltic Sea
Water Monitoring Program is closely related with Baltic States Helsinki Commission
(HELCOM) Water Monitoring Program.
The second program was initiated by the Klaipeda State Sea Port Authorities in 1995 and is
called the "Klaipeda Port Environmental Monitoring Program".
Several big companies have their own environmental monitoring programs, for example
'Naftos terminalas" is carrying out an Ecological Monitoring Program, and "Klaipedos vanduo"
is carrying out a monitoring program related to Drinking Water and Water Supply.
Two monitoring programs related to fishery resources (Coastal Zone Fish Resources
Monitoring Program and Salmon and Sea Trout Monitoring Program) are going on since 1995
and 1998 respectively. The responsible organisations are the State Fish Breading Centre
Laboratory for the first program and the freshwater research group "Centre for System
Analysis" of the Klaipeda University for the second.

4.7.4   Reception facilities for ship generated waste
Klaipeda is the only seaport of the Republic of Lithuania. The Port Authority, the state Oil
Enterprise and the state Stevedoring Company "Smelte" are the main institutions responsible
for the reception facilities in the Klaipeda Port. The port has adequate reception facilities for
oily waste, mixtures, ballast water and garbage to serve vessels visiting the port.
Oily waste from ships can be transported to purification facilities located in the State Oil
Enterprise of Klaipeda. The capacity of this treatment plant is 12 000 m3 per day or 4.38
million m3 per year. This plant is used within 10-20% of its capacity. Oily waste is pre-treated
using mechanical means only. Oil hydrocarbon concentrations in the treated water that is
discharged to the Baltic Sea vary between 0.5-1 mg/I.
The State Stevedoring Company "Smelte" owns the other purification facility for oily water
storage. This plant has a capacity of 301 m3 per day or 110 000 m3 per year. After local
purification, the wastewater is directed to the wastewater treatment plant of the Klaipeda
town, where at present only mechanical treatment will be applied. Biological treatment is
planned to be introduced in 1999.
Liquid garbage and sewage from ships are transported to the local mechanical treatment
Plant of "Smelte" Co. The capacity of this treatment plant is 4000 m3 per day or 1460
thousand m3 per year. After local purification, wastewater is transported to the wastewater
treatment plant of the Klaipeda town.
Oily sediments are transported to specially arranged town dumps. Oily rags and dry garbage
are to be burnt in a small incinerator. The other garbage is directed to the town garbage

4.7.5  Spill contingency (oil pollution) planning and response equipment
The Ministry of Environment of the Republic of Lithuania has the overall administrative and

behalf of the Klaipeda State Port Authority the operational response activities in relation to oil
pollution in the marine environment are executed by the Marine Rescue Co-ordination Centre

4.7.6  Fisheries in coastal and harbour area and Curonian lagoon
The commercial fishery is important both in the open Baltic Sea, the coastal zone area and
the northern part of Curonian lagoon. In the harbour area fishery is prohibited.
Recreational fishing also takes place but there are no statistical data available. In general it
can be stated that during good fishing weather conditions you can meet 500 - 2000 anglers in
this area.
TYPE OF FISHERIES, NUMBER OF BOATS, EMPLOYMENT
The types of commercial fishery are gillnetting, trawling, trapping and hooking. At present 64
boats are fishing in open Baltic Sea, whereas in the coastal area around 90 small fishing
companies with small boats operate. Baltic Sea fisheries involve approximately 3000 jobs.
The Curonian lagoon fishery relates to about 450 jobs more. Not included in these numbers
are the jobs related to fish processing and the fish trade industry.
In the lagoon approximately 20 small fishing companies employing around 80 fishermen
operate.
COMMERCIALLY IMPORTANT SPECIES
In Lithuanian economic zone and Lithuanian coastal zone the commercial fish resources
could be evaluated as follows:
Curonian lagoon:     1300-1500 metric ton,
Baltic Sea:          60000 - 70000 metric ton.
That means that about 97.5 % of total commercial fish resources of Lithuania are of marine
origin. In coastal zone 12 fish species are of interest to commercial fishery, of which 5 are
included in the Lithuanian Red Data Book and 10 species are migratory species.
The most important commercial species in the Baltic Sea area are smelt (Osmerus eperlanus
L.), flounder (Platichthys flesus trachurus Duncker), herring (Clupea harengus membras L.),

L.) and Sea Trout (Salmo trutta L.).
In the northern part of the lagoon the main commercial important species are smelt (Osmerus
eperlanus L.), pikeperch (Stizostedion lucioperca L.), bream (Abramis brama L.), roach

4.7.7   Recreation and water sports
The development of recreational activities and tourism in the coastal zone is mainly based on
the presence of natural resources like the Baltic sea, beautiful sandy beaches and the high
valued nature reserves "Kursiu Nerija National Park' and "Pajurio Landscape Park".
Practically the entire Curonian spit, as well as the sandy beaches to the north near Palanga
are well-known tourist attractions.
In the Giruliai-Melnrage area just to the north of Klaipeda and in the Smiltyne area at the
most northern part of the spit, several pensions, sanatoriums, hotels and children camps can
be found. During sunny days Smiltyne and Giruliai-Melnrage beaches are attended by up to
35000 and 26000 visitors per day respectively.
The most important recreational objects in this area are at Smiltyne: a marina, a maritime
museum and aquarium, a Dolphinarium and a Museum of the Nature of the Curonian spit.
At Giruliai-Melnrage: a gymnasticum, sauna's and pools.

5   ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT OF THE PROJECT DURING
OPERATION
5.1 IMPACT ON HYDRAULICS
Both the dredging works and the construction of new breakwaters will have a direct effect on
currents and waterlevels. The magnitude of these effects will vary depending on local
characteristics and conditions.
The opening at the port entrance will be considerably reduced due to the construction of the new
breakwaters. This will locally result in somewhat increased flow velocities. The dredging works in
the Klaipeda Port will reduce the hydraulic resistance of the Klaipeda Strait and hence will cause
more interchange of water between the Baltic Sea and the lagoon.
The flow in the Klaipeda Strait is alternately towards the sea and towards the lagoon. This flow is
mainly caused by wind set-up or set-down and the discharge of rivers into the lagoon. When the
flow is directed from the lagoon to the sea, the most recurrent water discharge in the Klaipeda
Strait is 1300 m3/s. During periods with increased water level differences between the lagoon and
the sea this discharge may increase to 3250 m3/s or in extreme situations even to 5000 m3/s.
Inflow from the sea to the lagoon on average occurs 40 times a year, with a total duration of 70
days. Usually the discharge during inflow from the sea is around 850 m3/s. However, during
extreme storms it could reach levels as much as 3500 m3/s.
Computer model computations by the Lithuanian Energy Institute (using the hydraulic model HEC-
RAS; Kaunas, 1998) and Harris (using the hydraulic model Duflow), indicate that as a
consequence of the planned dredging works the hydraulic resistance of the Klaipeda Strait will be
reduced. This will result in a 3 to 4% initial increase in discharge. The results of the discharge
calculations are presented in Table 5-1.
Table 5-1

affected by the project. However, at the lagoon side, both the increased inflow of water from the
sea and the faster outflow of lagoon water to the sea, will result in effects on water levels. In Table
5-2, these effects have been calculated for the most common and the most extreme flow regimes

differences between the lagoon and the sea.

conditions and to 33 cm in the most extreme conditions. Due to the dredging works an additional

importance.
In this respect it is to be noted, that in the LEI report (Kaunas, 1998) the effects of additional
deepening dredging works in other parts of the port have already been studied. The study showed
that this may lead to much stronger effects than found for this project.
