Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                         s
HARRIS
LONG WAVES
Long-wave action inside the port is dependent on the water depth at the harbour entrance. Water
depth in this case is the "large scale" undisturbed depth at the location of the entrance, i.e. without
taking into account the presence of the approach channel.
In the selected alternative, where the entrance is moved further away from the coast (i.e. to a
deeper "undisturbed" depth contour) it can be assumed that the iong-wave energy entering the
harbour will be reduced. In theory, this would be proportional to the water depth squared, but as
the long-wave theory is still not at a stage of having been proved, a conservative linear relation
with water depth is used. Based on this relationship the selected alternative would already yield a
reduction of about 25% in long-wave height compared to the existing situation.
Consequently it can be stated that the improved channel layout and the extensions of both
breakwaters will result in reduced wave heights in the port and improved navigational safety.
5.3 IMPACT ON SEDIMENTATION AND EROSION PROCESSES
The evaluation of the impact of a structure on the coast is only possible if the morphology of the
coastal system is understood. The coast can be affected in two ways, i.e.:
1. A structure blocks the net littoral drift and causes accretion on the updrift side and erosion on
the down drift side, and
2. Sand from the coast is trapped in a dredged channel and removed by maintenance dredging
and dumped outside the active coastal zone.
=ITTORAL DRIFT
With regard to item 1 the opinion on the littoral drift at the outlet of Klaipeda Strait is based on the
following information and considerations:
 The general situation and the history of the Curonian landspit suggests that it is a stable
beach barrier without significant sand supply, neither from the south (rocky headland at
Kaliningrad), nor from the north (not realistic considering the shape of the spiral beach and the
wave climate), nor from the rivers (sand trapped in the lagoon). The beach barrier very likely
is generated by cross-shore transport and further shaped and smoothed by forth and back
going longshore transport.
* A north going littoral drift of 400,000 m3/yr and no sand supply from the south would cause a
continuous retreat of the coastline of about 40 m per century near Zelenogradsk in the south.
The historic situation and the absence of reports on such erosion in spite of the many field
measurements in that area support the idea mentioned under item 1.
* The presence of an ice cover in a large area around the outlet during 57 days on average in
winter time will affect the actual wave climate. Very likely, this is not taken into account in
littoral drift computations. If taken into account by ignoring the wind data in winter time, this
could result in a lower figure for the net littoral drift.
*  The data on the composition of the sea bed-at the outlet of Klaipeda Strait do not suggest that
a considerable sand drift is passing in front of the outlet. Obviously, the sediment transport
generated by the in- and outflow of the outlet (mostly silt) dominates the littoral sand drift.
* The coastal impact of the breakwaters at the outlet seems to be very local. The impact seems
to be restricted to a distance of about 1000 m to the south of the outlet and about 1200 m to
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   5-3                       September1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project
HARRIS
the north. This corresponds to approximately two times the length of the breakwaters. The
coastlines of 1957,1990 and 1993 in this area show dynamic fluctuations rather than
systematic changes.
* Beyond these distances no distinct ongoing accretion south of the outlet is reported and no
distinct ongoing erosion north of it. Also this suggests that no distinct net littoral drift occurs.
* The difference in the slope of the foreshore on both sides of the outlet very well can be
explained by the oblique position of the breakwaters relative to the coast. This introduces
different diffraction patterns behind the breakwaters on both sides of the outlet. On the north
side diffraction will play a dominant role causing a steep slope of the foreshore (the waterline
can develop closer to the tip of the breakwater), whereas wave reflection on the breakwater
and rip currents may play a dominant role on the south side (keeping the waterline further
away from the tip of the breakwater). So, the different impacts on both sides are believed to
be caused by the asymmetry in the wave patterns rather than by the existence of a net
longshore sand drift.
*  The coasts adjacent to the outlet accreted on both sides in the period 1835-1878 as a result
of the breakwater construction. The sand for this accretion very likely came from the coast at
some greater distance and was drawn into the diffraction zones of the breakwaters. Some
erosion near the breakwaters was observed at least on the north side in the period 1878-
1957. There is no distinct reason to believe that the extension of the southern breakwater in
1957 had any effect on the coastal erosion. A more realistic explanation for the coastal
erosion is the loss of sand from the coast to the access channel to Klaipeda Port and
maintenance dredging.
X The migration of the outlet before it was fixed by the construction of the breakwaters not
necessarily is caused by north going sand drift. A very plausible explanation is the effect of
spiral flow in a bend, which causes migration in the direction of the outer bend. In rivers this
causes meandering.
From the above mentioned facts and arguments it is concluded that the net longshore sand drift is
negligible or small.
SAND Loss IN AccEss CHANNEL
A dredged channel passing the coastal zone acts as a disturbance of a natural system and
generates local gradients in the sediment transports. The reduced sediment transport capacity in
the deepened channel area makes it a sediment trap. Consequently, maintenance dredging is
required to maintain the nautical depth and width of the channel. An increased depth of the
dredged channel increases the trapping efficiency, because of the stronger flow reduction and
because the channel then becomes wider and longer. As a result the volume of required
maintenance dredging will increase.
In case the channel is not protected by breakwaters the littoral drift from either side will enter the
channel and will be the major cause of sedimentation. If the channel is maintained by regular
dredging and the spoil is dumped on land or in sea outside the active coastal zone, than it will
cause gradual erosion of the coast. Initially, this loss will be hardly noticeable because the sand
comes from long sections of the coast and the coastal regression will be very gradual.
In many cases the access channel is (partly) protected against (serious) sedimentation by the
construction of one or two breakwaters which block the littoral drift and prevent it to enter the
channel area. Generally, this protection is temporarily until sand starts bypassing the updrift
breakwater. Only in the case of zero net littoral sand drift the breakwaters can permanently
separate the channel area from the coastal zone, provided they are sufficiently long.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   5-4                         September'1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                         9
HARRIS
5.3.1  Impact of breakwater extension
The impact of the 315 m extension of the southern breakwater very much depends on the net
littoral drift along the Baltic coast of the landspit separating the Curonian lagoon from the Baltic
Sea. As mentioned in Section 4.3.3 a difference of opinion exists about the littoral drift in this area.
However, based on the arguments presented in the previous section it is likely that the longshore
sand drift is negligible or at least small.
So, blocking of net longshore sand drift is not at hand and the coasts on both sides of the outlet
can be considered as independent systems. Consequently, further extension of the southern
breakwater as planned will not affect the coastline in a significant way. Only some changes close
to the outlet may occur, particularly south of the outlet due to changes in the local wave conditions
there. The waves from NW-N will generate a somewhat different wave diffraction pattern and
waves from SW-W a somewhat different reflection pattern.
Also the possible extension of the northern breakwater will have no significant impact on the
coast, as it does not noticeably change the wave diffraction and reflection patterns north of the
entrance.
It is noted that in the present situation any net littoral drift (which is negligible or at least small) is
already trapped by the existing breakwaters and the deepened access channel. Further extension
of the breakwaters cannot make the situation worse anyhow.
5.3.2  Impact of deepening and widening the access channel
According to the historic data the access channel was deepened artificially for the first time in
1960-1962. The historic dredging data confirm the increase of maintenance dredging with
increasing channel depth. They also show that presently the clean spoil (from the outer channel)
is dumped north of the outlet at a rather great distance from the shore in water with a depth of 17
to 25 m.
The sandy character of the bottom of the outer channel proves that sedimentation will be largely
caused by sand from the coastal zone because the lagoon as a sand source is unlikely. The sand
can enter the channel area either from the north or from the south depending on the direction of
the incoming waves. It passes the breakwaters due to the combined effect of waves and flow
velocities in the entrance of Klaipeda Strait. After dredging the spoil, it presently is dumped in
rather deep water mostly outside the active coastal zone. Most of this the sand is lost for the
coastal system. Consequently, sooner or later, depending on the volume of sand that is annually
lost in this way, coastal erosion will occur.
It is believed that, in spite of the extensions of the breakwaters, the future deepening and widening
of the access channel will distinctly increase the annual maintenance dredging and with it the sand
loss from the coastal zone. It is believed that this will have a far more serious impact on the coast
than the extension of the southern breakwater. In fact, the latter will reduce extra sand loss from
the southern coast to the access channel. Initially, this sand loss from the coast will be hardly
noticeable because the sand comes from long sections of the coast on both sides of the outlet
and the coastal regression will be very gradual.
To reduce this negative impact it is advised to dump the clean sandy spoil from the access
channel as closely as possible near the beach at a distance of a few kilometres on both sides of
the outlet.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   5-5                       September1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                           HR
HARRIS
5.4 IMPACT ON SALT INTRUSION INTO THE LAGOON
The Curonian lagoon is a large water body with a total surface area of 1584 km2 . The southern
and central portions of the lagoon are freshwater due to discharge from the Nemunas River and
other smaller rivers. The predominant water flow is from the south to the north, i.e. in the direction
of the Klaipeda Strait and subsequently the sea.
However, the northern part of the lagoon and the Klaipeda Strait are influenced by seawater
intrusions from the Baltic Sea. These salt water (salinity 7%o) intrusions happen about 40 times
per year and usually last 1 to 2 days.
Sometimes intermediate conditions occur in the Strait. In those cases a fresh water surface layer
flows from the lagoon to the sea, whereas a salt water bottom layer flows from the sea to the
lagoon.
These rapid and irregular water exchanges cause large fluctuations in temperature, salinity and
other characteristics in the Klaipeda Strait and the northern part of the lagoon.
Based on the findings of long term monitoring it can be stated that salt water intrusions into the
lagoon strongly varies, but the sea always influences the Klaipeda Strait. In the winter salt water
could penetrate 20 or even 50 km north into the lagoon. Due to mixing salinity levels will be much
lower than at sea, but salinity levels of 0.06%o have been measured near Nida. During calm
weather periods in the summer, salt water penetrates into the Strait reaching salinity levels around
3.8%o.
The strongest salt water intrusion event since 1954 was recorded in 1977 when during September
of that year fresh water was only left in the Nemunas and Minija river mouths, while near
Juodkrante salinity levels reached 5.0 - 6.0%o.
Aquatic life has adapted to these more or less continuously changing environmental conditions.
Bottom macrofauna in the affected areas is permanently a mixture of marine, brackish and fresh
water species. The pelagic system (e.g. fish species) is characterised by the salinity levels and
can be divided into limnic (freshwater), frontal (brackish) and marine (salt water).
As the dredging works in the port will reduce the hydraulic resistance in the Strait (see Section
5.1) and consequently increase the exchange of water between the sea and the lagoon, an effect
on salt intrusion is eminent.
In a worst case scenario, the dredging works would lead to a 4% increase in the volume of salt
water into the lagoon. It is difficult to indicate the area affected, as depending on the conditions the
salt water will be mixed with fresh water and transported by currents to result in a variety of salinity
gradients. If all the additional salt water would remain together as one mega-drop, the area
affected by this volume of water would be approximately 1.3 km' and 5.6 km' for most common
inflow of 850 m3/s and the extreme condition of 3500 m /s respectively. On the basis of the total
surface area of the lake of 1584 km2, this would mean an insignificant area.
No doubt the dredging works will cause a slight increase in volume of salt water that will enter the
lagoon during intrusions. However, the volume increase is considered to be relatively small and
much smaller than the natural fluctuations. Consequently it is concluded that the dredging works
will not lead to significant changes in the salinity levels in the lagoon or to significant ecological
changes.
H7371    Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   5-6                         September1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                            9
HARRIS

Over the last 15 years four
major accidents occurred. In    Figure 5-1   Existing characteristics of

was wrecked and in one case
there was major damage to a quay and cranes. One of the accidents involved a tanker and
resulted in a large oil spill. Taking the traffic volume into consideration four major accidents in
such a short period is clearly too high.
Especially entering or departing loaded vessels of 20,000 DWT and larger compose a source of
potential danger. These ships must follow the relatively narrow channel itself and negotiate an
abrupt 230 bend, not wider then the channel itself, located directly at the entrance. When inbound
this leads to relatively high speeds in order to be able to compensate for wind, waves and current
while sailing within the channel limits. Slowing down or stopping occurs relatively close to the oil
terminals (Ni through N3).
Furthermore, the length of the breakwaters provides insufficient protection against waves to the
tugs to allow them to assist these vessels should assistance be required. Tugs, which are stand-
by to immediately provide assistance when an emergency situation develops, are called escort
tugs. Escorting generally requires dedicated tugs, which can operate in higher waves and at
higher speeds than tugs assisting manoeuvres such as berthing. The tugs, which are presently
available, are unable to assist in significant wave heights over 1 m and are therefore not
considered as escorting tugs.
If tankers must leave the oil terminals under emergency conditions, only a relatively small distance
is available to accelerate to a sufficiently high speed and an adequate course to depart safely from
the sheltered harbour area.
The wind is the main determining condition. The wind is the driving force behind waves and
currents. Therefore the wind force and its direction are the environmental variables determining
the limits for entry and departure. Additionally the size of the vessel, the position of the berth in
question and the direction of the manoeuvre (entry or departure) are of influence on the
acceptable environmental limits. The position of the berth is of influence, as the wave height at the
H7371    Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   5-7                         September1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                               s
HARRIS
berths becomes lower if the position of the berths is further into the port. As the operational limit of
the significant wave height of the tugs is presently 1 m, tugs may be able to operate at the
KLASCO port and inner port, while not being able to operate at the oil berths.
Independent of the direction or force of the wind, swell may exist in rare cases. Arriving ships will
not enter if the swell is over 2.5 m in height. Ships will not depart if the swell is over 3 m in height.
Vessels below 7000 tons dead-weight have a draught less tha,n 7 m and these ships are not
bound to the channel limits. In the case that a ship is not bound to the channel limits, the
manoeuvring area increases to such an extent that more favourable tracks and headings can be
followed than possible within the channel limits.
In its present state the nautical safety of the port is below any international standard.
THE PLANNED SITUATION
Mitigating measures aiming primarily at improving the nautical safety for ships entering and
leaving the port have been studied by Delft Hydraulics and MSCN (1996-1997). For detailed
descriptions of the alternative designs and suggested mitigation measures reference is made to
these reports.
In total eight different possible
harbour entrance layouts were                         :.!    ...........

ADDITIONAL STOPPING LENGTH AND BETTER WAVE PROTECTION THROUGH EXTENSION OF BREAKWATERS
Ships approaching a harbour under near limiting environmental conditions must sail with a certain
speed. This is so because wind, waves and current would push the ship out of the channel at low
speeds. In order to counter these effects-the.ship sails under an angle (drift angle) with the
channel axis. The more severe the conditions, the more speed the ship needs to stay in the
channel. Large drift angles form a potential source of danger, because sudden changes in the
external conditions (for instance a sudden drop in wind or a current gradient) can make the ship
collide with the channel boundaries.
H7371    Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   5-8                         September1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                              R
HARRIS
Wind and waves create a moment on ships. Vessels in loaded condition experience a moment
due to the superstructure aft on the ship. This effect is less pronounced or even absent if the
ships are in ballast or when the wind area stretches evenly over the entire length as is the case for
container vessels and ro-ro vessels. To counter the wind-moment effect the ship must sail with a
more or less constant rudder angle. This means that there is less "spare" rudder angle available
for the ship to use to negotiate a turn or in an emergency. Again under more severe conditions,
more speed is needed to maintain sufficient pressure on the rudder.
Generally, the environmental forces become larger with increasing vessel size. This is even more
unpleasant because the size of the rudder and the propulsion increase less than proportionally
with the size of the vessel (especially larger bulk carriers and oil tankers), i.e. large vessels are
less manoeuvrable and need long distances to stop.
When stopping a ship, the propeller must produce astern thrust while the ship is still going ahead.
In this situation the flow around the rudder is so confused that the rudder is totally ineffective.
At present the available stopping length for ships entering Klaipeda is relatively short and the
stopping manoeuvre takes place close to the oil berths. This is undesirable because (as explained
above) it is impossible to control a ship when it is decelerating without tug assistance. Tug
assistance may be difficult to provide because there is often too much wave action for the tugs to
be effective. Consequently creating additional protected length beyond the present breakwater
limits which means extension of (both) the breakwaters will certainly improve navigational safety.
Other positive effects of the extensions are:
*  The bend will now be located further inside the harbour entrance and outside the area of
cross-current. This means that the ship requires less use of rudder increasing the safety
margins.
*  Under operational conditions, ships of the same size approaching the harbour will be able to
reduce their speed to under 6 knots (present speed limit inside the harbour) when passing the
oil terminals.
*  With the additional wave protection the tugs will now be able to pull the ship from the (oil)
berths in case of an emergency or when the ship starts exhibiting motions which are above
the acceptable limits. The latter increases the flexibility of the port because otherwise ships
have to leave berth long before this is necessary from a motions point of view, as otherwise
the tugs will not be able to assist them. Wave penetration at the oil berths will be reduced
from about 60% in the existing situation (west and north west wave directions) to about 35 %
after implementation of the breakwater extension (see Section 5.2). This means that tugs can
be fully effective under all required conditions.
*  Ships having to leave under emergency conditions will have sufficient room to accelerate to
about seven knots without using a "harbour full" telegraph setting and will be able to safely
leave the harbour by further increasing the pressure on the rudder with a higher telegraph
setting.
*  The increased wave protection reduces the motions of the moored ships.
A distinct disadvantage of the extended breakwaters is an increase (by approximately 30%) in the
current velocity across the harbour entrance.
WIDENING OF CHANNEL
As mentioned above a ship sails through a channel with a drift angle in order to counter wind,
waves and current. The narrower the channel is, the smaller the drift angle will have to be in order
to keep the ship inside the channel boundaries, while maintaining certain safety margins.
Decreasing a drift angle can only be achieved by increasing the ship's speed, which is not always
desirable from a safety point of view especially when entering the harbour.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental ImpactAssessment   5-9                          September1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                            ml
HARRIS
The width of the first leg of the present channel is not more than 100 m. This is less than the
recommendation by PIANC for mild conditions and one way traffic. The PIANC recommends the
width of a channel equal to at least five times the width of the design vessel. In addition, the
environmental conditions at Klaipeda must be classified as moderate.
The full mission real time manoeuvring simulations show that the required channel width for
vessels up to 30,000 DWT is 140 m.
Thle width of the secondA leg is 75 m. Even though the wave ciimate along this leg of the channel
can be classified as mild, the local wind climate must be classified as moderate. Therefore, the
width of the second leg of the channel must be at least equal to the first leg of the channel.
A wider channel means improved safety because:
* when sailing with the same speed as in a narrower channel there is more "spare" room in
case of emergencies, and/or
* when using (part of) the additional channel width the ship can sail with lower speeds, thus
increasing safety when entering the harbour.
IMPROVEMENT OF THE BEND IN THE CHANNEL
In the existing situation (see Figure 5-1) the channel has a bend of some 230 just inside the port
entrance. This exposes the bend to much wave agitation, which is undesirable from a
manoeuvring point of view. Besides, the channel at this point is not wider than the channel itself,
which is also undesirable from a manoeuvring point of view.
The bend itself will allow the large majority of ships to follow their natural tendency to turn to
starboard during stopping. Besides, the increased channel surface area at the location of the bend
allows the ships some space to drift sideways. Therefore they can maintain astern revolutions
longer and also can profit from the fact that stopping distances of ships decrease considerably if
the ship is allowed to drift sideways.
REALIGNMENT OF INNER CHANNEL
The close proximity of the northern shore to the channel is undesirable for two reasons:
1. Safety considerations based on the nature of the cargo transferred at these berths require as
much distance as possible between traffic in the channel and the berthed ships.
2. Traffic is permitted to pass the berths at a speed of 6 knots. This puts considerable strain on
the mooring cables of the berthed ships and therefore constitutes a danger to the berthed
ships and the cargo transfer operations.
The best option for the orientation of the second leg of the channel is to have it parallel to the axis
of the harbour entrance. At the moment it narrows towards the northern shore.
Therefore, it is required to realign the inner channel to the south. The inner channel will then be
located further away from the existing berths which increases the safety and will also be located in
the protected area of the breakwaters, allowing for better tug assistance.
CREATION OF STOPPING AREA WITH ENOUGH WIDTH
For ships performing a stopping manoeuvre it is impossible to control their heading without the
assistance of tugs or bow thrusters. During the stopping manoeuvre ships have a natural
tendency to turn to starboard (right hand turning propellers) or to port (left hand turning
)         propellers). Caution should be exercised, as the tendency to turn due to the effect of the propeller
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   5-10                        September 999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                            s
HARRIS
is not always predictable. Also, under the influence of wind the ships will be set to starboard or
port.
Ships cannot control these effects because during the stopping their rudder is ineffective.
Therefore the channel has to be widened locally in the stopping area to create the necessary
space to allow the ships to drift during stopping.
The required width of the stopping area is approximately 1.5 times the channel width, which will
give sufficient space and will even allow the ship to turn in the channel if necessary.
Summarising it can be stated that the planned new layout with an improved inner and outer
access channel and extended breakwaters, which will give better protection against wave action
to the inner channel and the oil and Klasco berths, will result in improved navigational conditions
and safety.
For specific information about the exact design characteristics for both the dredging and the
breakwaters reference is made to the related design documents.
5.6 IMPACT ON ENVIRONMENTAL RISKS
In its present state the nautical safety of the port is below any international standard, and accident
risks clearly exist not only in theory, but also in practice.
The Oil Terminal berths are located too close to the entrance channel creating a risk that a
passing vessel will collide with oil tankers at berth, causing an oil spill. This situation is even more
critical under adverse sea and weather conditions.
Under the present configuration of the entrance channel, when the external wave heights exceed
one meter, tugs cannot provide assistance to ships beyond the breakwater heads. This is due to
insignificant reduction in wave height at that location and inability of tugs to operate in wave
heights exceeding one meter. It creates another risk of oil spill since a non-tug-assisted tanker or
other ship may hit the breakwaters.
The modification of the existing breakwaters, proposed under the project, will result in a reduction
of the distance between the breakwater heads to reduce wave disturbance inside the port. This
will increase navigational safety considerably. Besides, the increased width and depth of the
entrance channel, as well the re-alignment of the second leg (see Section 5.5) will also greatly
improve the navigational safety of the port. Consequently, the project will result in reduced risks of
accidents and oil spills.
5.7  IMPACT ON AQUATIC LIFE
REDIRECTION OF OUTFLOW OF THE LAGOON
It may be foreseen that the area effected by the oufflow of the Lagoon will be changed due to
extension of the breakwaters. It's likely that the oufflow, which now is often directed along the
shore by prevailing currents and westerly winds, after the reconstruction will take a position further
to the sea.
Consequently, it may be expected that the number of days when the Lagoon's water occupies the
near shore areas will decrease. This may result in improvement of light conditions in the coastal
biotopes. Now the outflow is considered to be one of the most important factors, which depress
development of benthic vegetation in these areas. The lagoon's water contains huge amounts of
suspended material and phytoplankton, especially in summer and early autumn. Therefore it is
probable that general conditions for the stony bottoms vegetation north of the port entrance (i.e.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   5-11                        september1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project
HARRIS
red algae) may improve. If this happens the territory of spawning grounds suitable for e.g. Baltic
herring may increase too.
After the extension of the breakwaters the oufflow of water will not only be further in sea, but also
its initial direction will change from north-west to west. Depending on local sea currents this may
result in an effect on water quality south of the port entrance. Consequently, the Klaipeda Maritime
Aquarium, which takes in seawater at this point, may face water quality problems.
NEW SUBSTRATES
The extension of the breakwaters will create new substrates for benthic life. As a result the area of
rocky hard substrates (concrete, rocks and tetrapods) will increase approximately for 30-40 %.
These substrates are especially important for benthic vegetation (mainly filamentous green algae),
sessile animals (barnacles, mussels, etc.) and associated mobile fauna like benthopelagic
crustaceans and demersal fishes. Total filtration capacity of the barnacles and mussels dwelling
on the newly extended breakwaters will increase too. New artificial biotopes will serve as
additional spawning grounds for Baltic herring and other fish. Locally, at the breakwaters, the
biodiversity of marine life will increase.
RISK ASSOCIATED WITH INCREASED VOLUMES OF SHIPS BALLAST WATERS
As a consequence of the project it is to be expected that ship traffic from and to the port will
increase. The increase in size and number of ships entering the port will result in increased risk of
alien species invasions. This risk is associated with ballast waters, which are released from the
ships in the vicinity of, or inside the port. Proper measures (see appropriate IMO guidelines)
should be taken in order to minimise this risk. This is especially important for the Lagoon and
Klaipeda Strait, which already harbour 18 alien species.
CHANGES IN THE LAGOON ECOSYSTEM
As the dredging works in Klaipeda strait will increase, the total area of temporal defaunated
benthic zones will increase too. Assuming that the volume of inflowing marine waters will not
increase significantly (see Section 5.4), the changes in the species composition in the lagoon are
not expected. However the densities of species of marine origin may slightly increase. Also the
area of their penetration into the Lagoon is likely to extend for some kilometres. Consequently the
frontal (transitory) zone which is relatively rich in species will widen too.
Significant changes in plankton communities are not expected since the water exchange pattern
(i.e. seasonal changes and inflow frequency) is not altered.
5.8  IMPACT ON FISHERIES
Besides the positive effect of the new breakwaters in relation to the increased surface area for
spawning and shelter, no significant effects on fisheries are expected.

6   ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT OF THE PROJECT DURING
CONSTRUCTION
The most dominant construction activities of the project are the dredging and disposal
operations, and the construction of the new breakwaters. Both construction activities can have a
variety of impacts on the environment and are discussed in this section in relation to the
following possible impact areas:
* dredging and disposal on water quality, including turbidity (6.1)
* the construction of the breakwaters on water quality (6.2)
* on aquatic life (6.3)
* on fisheries (6.4)
* dredging on the stability of the existing breakwaters (6.5)
* on navigation and port operations (6.6)
* on urban land traffic (6.7)
* on recreation (6.8)
* pollution caused by the equipment used (6.9)
6.1   IMPACT OF DREDGING AND DISPOSAL ON WATER QUALITY
The increase in turbidity during dredging and disposal operations is one of the most important
factors of the physical impact on water quality. It is the visible cloudiness associated with
turbidity that can cause considerable unfavourable public response to some dredging projects.
Increased levels of suspended sediments can also effect aquatic ecology and water intake
systems. The aspects of resuspension of sediments and increased turbidity levels will be
discussed in more detail below.
Among the important chemical impacts are changes in oxygen concentration, uptake and
release of nutrients and uptake and release of toxins. It is generally recognised that some
oxygen loss will occur when any sediment is exposed to oxygenated water, but the magnitude of
the loss will depend on the particular sediment and the chemical and physical factors in the
dredging and disposal environment. In view of the relatively low production rates and the open
character of the water body, these effects are expected to be negligible.
The quality of the sediments to be dredged has been investigated (see Section 4.4.3). The
laboratory results showed that the majority of the sediments, i.e. all the sediments in the
Worldbank dredging area and the glacial till sediments in the entire project area, are clean or
only slightly contaminated. Significant release of contaminants from these sediments is therefore
not to be expected. However, a part of the sediments of the project area, i.e. the soft surface
sediments in the LEI dredging area, are contaminated (class 3 level of contamination according
Lithuanian classification system). In the LEI report it is indicated that the quantity of
contaminated sediments is relatively small and that there is no objection to dump these
sediments at sea. Consequently no special precautions will be taken in this respect, not in
relation to the selection of the dredging -techn-ique, nor in relation to the disposal of these
sediments.
It is the viewpoint of the Worldbank study team, that this approach is not in line with the concept
of environmental protection, or the international attempts to improve the water quality of the
Baltic Sea. Under the Baltic Sea Joint Comprehensive Environmental Action Programme,
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   6-1                      September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                         0
HARRIS
Klaipeda was identified as one of the pollution "hot spots" in Lithuania. Measures to improve the
water quality in and around Klaipeda are therefore listed as priority actions under the Baltic Sea
Programme approved by the regional Ministers of Environment at Helsinki (April 1992) and
Gdansk (March 1993).
Both the planned dredging and the disposal of contaminated sediments into the Baltic Sea will
have a negative effect on marine life and fisheries. Depending on the location of dumping, a part
of the contaminated sediments might even end up on the beaches. The problem of the
contaminated sediments and options for a more environmentally sound approach to this problem
are discussed in Section 7.2 of this EIA.
6.1.1   Background Information on Turbidity
One of the most visible aspects of dredging and open water disposal is the resuspension of
sediments and resulting increased turbidity levels. These two parameters are not identical.
Turbidity is related to the transmission of light through a liquid medium, whereas suspended
sediments are related to the weight of the solids in the water. However, the two are interrelated
and for a specific site a relationship between the two can be established.
It was long thought that the resuspension of sediments and visible turbidity plumes had a very
negative effect on water quality and aquatic life. Therefore, in the past two decades a large
number of studies were conducted by various researchers to assess the basics of resuspension
of sediments (turbidity generation), as well as the physical, chemical and biological impacts on
the aquatic environment. The results of these studies indicate that all conventional dredgers
generate turbidity in the vicinity of the dredger to a varying degree depending on the type of the
dredger, the characteristics of the sediment and the dredging and disposal methods used.
However, the turbidity is usually of a short duration and normal conditions are restored in a short
time. Furthermore many studies indicated that turbidity generated by natural causes (e.g.
currents, waves, floods and storms), are often quite high and affect a much larger area when
compared to dredging operations.
During the past decade the attention to resuspension of sediments has increased in view of the
fact that more and more sediments were found to be contaminated. Consequently several
measures were developed to reduce turbidity in order to prevent contaminants from spreading
(see also Section 7.2).
RESUSPENSION AND DISPERSION
The nature, degree, and extent of dredged material dispersion around a dredging or disposal
operation are controlled by many factors, including:
*  the quantity and characteristics of the sediments, like grain size distribution, solids
concentration, etc.
*  the type and size of the dredger and the dredging/disposal techniques and the operational
procedures used.
*  the local environmental conditions, including salinity, currents, waves, tides, storms, floods,
etc.
Generation of turbidity and its retention is greatly affected by the settling velocity of the sediment
particles, which in turn depends on the size of the particles. When the sediment size is less than
50 pm (1 pm =1 micron = 0.001 mm) in diameter, the settling velocity of the particle follows
)         Stoke's law. When the particle size is greater than 50 pm, it follows Rubey's law. In Stoke's law
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   6-2                      September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                                              HRI
HARRIS
the settling velocity is proportional to the square of the diameter of the sediment particle,
whereas in Rubey's law the settling velocity is proportional to the square root of the particle
diameter. In any case, the larger the size of the particle the faster the settling rate.
Figure 6-1 gives the relationship between the particle size and the settling velocity. Using the
relationship of this figure, the distance travelled by a particle of a specific size under the
influence of a current can be determined. The formula for the travel distance is:
Distance travelled = 0.6 D T V meters

V = current velocity in cm/sec

For example:
A sediment particle of 100 pm (fine sand) released at the surface of the project area (12 meters
of water depth and current velocity of 10 cm/sec in the outer sea channel) will travel only 144
meters (0.6 x 12 x 2 x 10 = 144), before it is back on the bottom. Coarser sand of 200 pm will
resettle within approximately 3 minutes and will travel no further than approximately 50 meters.
In this simplification, the effects of turbulence on falling particles, variations in vertical density
and temperature have been ignored. Also the current velocity has been considered to be uniform
over the vertical plane. In the project area considerable variations in magnitude and direction of
currents are experienced at different depths. It is however interesting to note that using the
average vertical current velocity on the vertical results in a rather accurate value of the distance
travelled.
H7371     Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment  6-3                                          September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                             I
HARRIS
So, although this relationship greatly simplifies the behaviour of sediment particles in the water,
it does demonstrate why the vast majority of sediment particles resettles quite quickly and why
turbidity plumes are only found rather close to the dredger.
Obviously the smaller the particles the longer they will stay in suspension and the further they will
travel. However flocculation is another important factor in sediment motion and is a process by
which fine particles suspend in a fluid combine (floc) to form a mass of particles and eventually
settle to the bottom. The great majority of particles involved in flocculation are clays. Clays
flocculate most readily when the pH is less than 7, which is the case in the study area.
Given a set of environmental conditions, different types of dredgers generate different levels of
turbidity. Though the type of dredging equipment has a major effect on the amount and
concentration of sediment that is resuspended, the techniques for operating this equipment are
considered equally important. This has become very clear during the recent studies on dredging
contaminated sediments in the Netherlands and the United States. With dredging contaminated
sediments the resuspension of contaminated sediments and possible release of contaminants
have to be minimised. Several studies have demonstrated that the resuspension of sediments,
using the same dredger, varies considerably depending on the operational methods used.
During the past five years 22 dedicated monitoring campaigns on resuspension of sediments
were carried out in the Netherlands, with different types of dredgers and different types of
sediments, including clays, silts and sands. The results confirmed the results of similar studies
carried out in the United States, that the amount of sediments resuspended during dredging
ranges from 0 to 5% of the volume dredged. However, it was also clearly demonstrated that this
amount is not directly related to the type of dredger. Using a grab dredger the percentage of
resuspended material ranged from 0.5 to 5%, whereas using a trailing suction hopper dredger
the percentage ranged from 0.2 to 4.5 %. The operational technique used and the local
characteristics play an important role.
It should be remembered that though activities like dredging and disposal are contributing to the
increase in turbidity and suspended material levels, there are several other natural and man-
made causes that generate considerable amounts of turbidity in open water, e.g. currents,
waves, tides, storms, seasonal floods, shipping traffic, fisheries activities, etc. In many cases
turbidity caused by storms and waves is quite high.
In the Klaipeda Strait the natural concentrations of suspended sediments throughout the year
fluctuate from 3 to more than 60 mg/I. Suspended sediment levels in the nearshore area are
lower, ranging from 2 to more than 20 mg/I. However these data are based on calm weather
conditions. Unfortunately no data are available for stormy periods, to demonstrate the
sometimes enormous effect of natural causes on turbidity levels in the study area. Especially in
estuaries and river systems turbidity is not distributed evenly over the water column, resulting in
considerable differences between two nearby locations. Figure 6-2 shows an example of the
differences in turbidity levels near the head of the southern breakwater during calm weather
conditions.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    6-4                       September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                         HRI
HARRIS
Figure 6-2   Sediment transport near South breakwater head
6.1.2  Evaluation of the dredging induced resuspension of sediments
In the project area, the following three types of dredging activities are foreseen (although it is
mentioned that the selected contractor will determine the final choice of the dredging
equipment):
*a sand layer will be removed by a trailing suction hopper dredger.
*a boulder clay layer will be dredged by a backhoe dredger
*a sandy/silt layer and a boulder clay layer will excavated by a bucket dredger
With a trailing suction hopper dredger a suction pipe directly excavates the bottom sediments.
The excavated material is picked up and pumped by a centrifugal pump into the ships own
hopper. The majority of the sand particles will settle in the hopper, whereas excess water and a
part of the fine sediment particles will be discharged back into the water via the so-called
overflow, resulting in resuspended sediments and turbidity. This process is continued till the
hopper is sufficiently loaded. The sand layer outside the present breakwaters contains relatively
little silt. Consequently, the largest portion of the resuspended sediments will resettle in the
vicinity of the dredger. The finer particles will stay longer in suspension and may travel further
along the coast. At a distance of approximately 500 meters of the dredger, suspended sediment
levels will have dropped to levels below that of a normal storm. In this respect it is to be noted
that under stormy conditions resuspension and displacement of sediments can cause bottom
levels changes in this area of over 1 meter.
Dredging of the boulder clay in this offshore area will probably be carried out with a backhoe
dredger. The very hard boulder clay is not easily erodable and consequently excavation of this
type of sediment is not expected to result in large resuspensions of sediments. No doubt to
some extent resuspension will occur. This will be mainly at the bottom due to the excavation
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   6-5                        September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                          H
HARRIS
process and at the surface due to the overflow of the barges during loading. This latter effect can
be minimised by putting restrictions on allowed overflow.
Dredging of the boulder clay inside the port area with a bucket dredger will more or less result in
the same effects as with the backhoe. However, in this area a sandy silt layer will be dredged as
well. It is anticipated that the removal of this layer will lead to more resuspension of sediments.
Especially in those areas where the sediments are contaminated this may lead to negative
effects on the environment (dispersion of contamination). Consequently, resuspension and
dispersion of contaminated sediments should be prevented as much as possible. No doubt this
will require adaptation of the dredging methodology.
Where the resuspended sediments will resettle will mainly depend on the current conditions and
water turbulence. A part of the resuspended sediments may settle on the stones of the
breakwaters. During certain parts of the year these breakwaters function as spawning places for
the Baltic herring, and settling of sediments may smother the eggs. According the rules of the
Minister of the Environment it is already foreseen that during these periods dredging in this area
is restricted (also see Section 6.4).
Based on the above, and the fact that these sediments are not contaminated, it is to be
expected that these dredging works will only have a small and temporary effect on water quality.
Natural conditions will be restored within hours after the dredging works have stopped.
6.1.3  Evaluation of the disposal induced resuspension of sediments
Two types of disposal are foreseen in relation to the planned dredging works, i.e. nearshore
disposal of sandy sediments and offshore disposal for the remaining sediments.
NEARSHORE DISPOSAL OF SAND
Investigations by the LEI have revealed that the sand dredged from the entrance channel of the
Klaipeda port would be fit for beach and dune ridge recultivation in the Curonian spit, Melnrage II
- Karkle and Nemirsieta sectors. This sand would also be helpful in forming the absolutely
degraded foredune in the sector between the northern breakwater and Melnrage I. As the beach
inclinations in these sectors are sufficiently great, the waves would only reach the ridge during
extreme storms. If reinforced with plants this foredune ridge will remain functional for a long time.
Recultivation of the area would be optimal, if the grain sizes of the dredged sediments are the
same as those in the naturally developed coastal sector. Smaller particles, as a rule, form a
nearshore with a smaller inclination. Besides, they are gradually carried down slope to greater
depths. Particles of greater size are gradually buried in the mass of smaller particles. However,
they are more fit for recultivation than the small ones.
An eroding coast is one of the possible ecological problems related with the deepening of the
Klaipeda port till the depth of 14 m (see Section 5.3). By relocating the dredged sediments within
the coastal system, both for the capital dredging works and the following maintenance dredging
works, this problem -is expected to be solved completely. This would also be the most rational
and cheap way of stabilising the coastline in the7Impact zone of the Klaipeda port.
In relation to this project two types of coast recultivation can be used: beach/dune replenishment
and foreshore replenishment.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    6-6                       September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                             I
HARRIS
With beach/dune replenishment sand is brought onto the beach. This can be done in a variety of
ways: e.g. by pipeline directly into the work or to a stockpile, by "rainbowing" from the trailing
suction hopper dredger, etc.
With foreshore replenishment sand is placed at the shallow foreshore. This can be done by
pipeline, but most commonly by direct dumping from the hopper or barges.
With beach replenishment the amount of suspended sediment released in the water is less than
with foreshore replenishment, as the sand is washed into the sea in smaller amounts and at
slower rates. However, both techniques are regularly used all over the world and seldom lead to
negative environmental effects. Also for this project, the beneficial use of clean sandy sediments
is not expected to have significant detrimental environmental effects.
OFFSHORE DISPOSAL OF NON-CONTAMINATED AND CONTAMINATED DREDGE SEDIMENTS
At present all sediments dredged from the Klaipeda port are transported to marine disposal site
No. 3, which is located some 20 kilometres offshore. This disposal site is operational since 1987.
Investigations have shown that this dumpsite is located in a lithodinamically active zone. It was
found that material deposited here will rapidly spread over a substantial area. Sediment will even
be transported to the nearshore zone, where it will deposit at a depth of 20 - 25 m.
For the present project it is foreseen to dump all dredged sediments here, with the exception of
the re-usable sands.
The boulder clay is rocklike material and is likely to stay more or less at the site where it is
dumped. Over time the boulder clay will disintegrate and silt particles will be washed out. In view
of the water depth (>20 m) and the low current velocities this is expected to be a very slow
process. However, silty sediments are also dumped at this location and these sediments are
indeed expected to spread over a wider area.
As long as these sediments are not contaminated the effects on the aquatic environment are
relatively small and of a temporary nature. It goes without saying that the disposal of
contaminated silty sediment will result in a wide area being negatively affected. Besides, disposal
of contaminated sediments in open water will have a negative effect on water quality, as during
the dumping process a part of the contaminants will be released to the water column.
6.2  IMPACT OF CONSTRUCTION OF BREAKWATERS ON WATER QUALITY
The new breakwaters will be of the rubble mound type, with various layers of rock and concrete
material. The core of the breakwaters will be constructed by dumping quarry run or gravel using
dump barges and profiling the material using a pontoon mounted grab. Both quarry run and
gravel contain a certain volume of fine particles, which during dumping may resuspend in the
water column. Depending on the environmental characteristics at the place and time of dumping
(wave conditions, currents, etc.) the resuspended sediments will disperse to a wider area. As no
data are available regarding the quantity of fines in these building materials, little can be said
about the scale of this effect. In general however the quantities of fines in these materials are
rather small and the effects on water quality are small and of short duration.
6.3   IMPACT ON AQUATIC LIFE
Basically dredging and the disposal of dredged material and to a lesser degree breakwater
construction, constitute a process of artificially induced sediment erosion, transport and
deposition. The process differs from the natural one in being much more concentrated in time
H7371    Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    6-7                      September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                          n
HARRIS
and space. Many organisms are not able to either adapt quickly or leave the affected
environment and consequently dredging and disposal will have an effect on aquatic life.
Dredging and disposal of bottom sediments can cause a variety of ecological impacts, but are
basically related to:
* removal of bottom sediments
* increased turbidity and changes in water quality
* bottom sediment build-up
The latter two also apply to breakwater construction, be it that with bottom sediment build up, the
construction of the breakwater is meant.
To scale the effects it is important to assess which part of the aquatic environment will be
affected, based on surface coverage as well as on ecological values. As most species live near
the water sediment surface (the so-called life-layer), the effects on benthic fauna will approxi-
mately be proportional to the surface area disturbed.
Secondly, it is important to differentiate between temporary and more long-term effects.
Increased levels of turbidity and changes in water quality are directly related to the actual
dredging and disposal/breakwater fill activities and will stop when these activities are stopped.
Removal of bottom material (at the dredging site), as well as bottom sediment build up (at the
disposal site), will have a longer lasting effect on benthic life, as it may take nature from one to
several years to recover by means of recolonisation.
Thirdly, it is important to realise that changes in the aquatic environment and especially in
coastal areas are normal phenomena: In the period 1746-1796 the Curonian Spit became 565 m
longer by natural processes. Water was transformed into land and land into water. During
periods of strong storms large volumes of marine sediments are transported into the Strait.
Sometimes the thickness of the sediment layer deposited exceeds 1 m in certain parts of the
harbour. In neither of these events, the loss of aquatic habitats is considered an environmental
problem.
REMOVAL OF BOTTOM SEDIMENTS
It will be evident that the present bottom in the dredging area, inclusive the organisms living in
and on the so-called life-layer (top 20 cm) will be removed. The total area affected by dredging is
approximately 1 kM2, of which 20% is located in the sea and 80% in the Klaipeda Strait. The
ecological value of the bottom in this area is limited, as a large part belongs to the existing
navigational channel where maintenance dredging is carried out on a regular basis, whereas
another part is covered with contaminated sediments.
Consequently the effect of the removal of the top layer of the bottom by dredging can be
considered of minor importance.
The sea area covered by the new breakwaters is negligible when compared to the remaining
coastal area.
INCREASED TURBIDITY AND CHANGES IN WATER QUA[lTY
As discussed in Section 6.1, the dredging and construction works will affect water quality. In the
vicinity of the dredgers, at the disposal site and at the breakwater construction site turbidity
levels will increase. The effects of increased turbidity are local and of a short-term nature.
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    6-8                       September 1999



K(laipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                          ARS
HARRIS       
However in view of the presence of artificial spawning areas on the breakwaters, special care is
required (see 6.4)
BoTToM SEDIMENT BUILD-UP
At the sites where the breakwaters will be constructed, construction materials will cover the
existing bottom sediments. At the disposal site(s) sediments will be deposited on top of the
present bottom. In view of the characteristics of the sediments dumped at the offshore disposal
site, the original more or less flat seabed will be transformed into an area showing randomly
distributed  mounts of deposited sediments. In all related areas local benthic life will be
destroyed.
Besides, at the offshore disposal site and at the breakwater construction sites, the original
bottom is/was composed of sandy sediment, which will be replaced by more clayey sediments
and/or rock. These changes in sediment characteristics will affect the composition of the benthic
fauna during recolonisation. Recolonisation of the disposal site is expected to be slow.
EVALUATION OF THE IMPACTS
The effects of the construction works on aquatic life in the project area are considered small.
The effects of turbidity and suspended sediments will not cause significant or long-lasting effects
to the aquatic life. The dredging and the disposal areas will recover by recolonisation, but this
process is expected to take several years. The dredging activities might endanger or destroy the
spawning areas at the existing breakwaters if special precautions are not followed.
6.4   IMPACT ON FISHERIES
DREDGING AREA
The whole study area is practically free from natural spawning grounds for commercial fish.
However, the breakwaters serve as artificial spawning grounds for herring (see Section 4.6.1).
Besides, practically all year round in the Klaipeda Strait, being the only direct link between the
Baltic Sea and the Curonian lagoon, both anadromic (from the sea to the lagoon) and
catadromic (from the lagoon to the sea) migration takes place.
The most important periods for fish spawning migration are from April 1 to May 31 and from
August 16 to October 31.
Both dredging and the construction of the breakwaters may have detrimental effects on the
breakwater spawning areas and migrating fish, due to increased levels of resuspended
sediments. Especially smothering of the roe by settling sediment particles is expected to have a
very negative effect.
To safeguard both the artificial spawning grounds and the fish spawning migration, the ministry
of Environmental Protection has issued an order in relation to this topic called "On the
assessment of the effects of Klaipeda port dredging works on fishery". The full text of this Order
is included in Appendix B to this report.
The order states that dredging in the port, in the period of fish spawning migration (from April 1
to May 31 and from August 16 to October 31), shall only be done in case of urgent necessity and
provided that the following conditions are met:
* A plan of the dredging works must be prepared and co-ordinated with the Department of
Fish Supplies before the beginning of the works. The plan must contain information about
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment    6-9                       September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                        H
HARRIS
concrete dredging sites, terms and range and provide measures for reducing the negative
effects on fish migration.
* In the course of dredging works the port, by its own expenses must carry out a monitoring
investigation of migration intensity and dredging works effects on migrating fish. If there is a
necessity certain corrections of the dredging works must be made in 24 hours. The
investigations must be carried out by specialists who are not subordinate to the port.
The Order also states that if dredging is carried out during the given period as a consequence of
necessity, a damage fee is to be paid for each m3 dredged. However, the damage fee is so low
(less than 1% of the normal m3 price), that it is not likely that this by itself will have an inhibiting
effect on dredging activities.
In view of the damage to fisheries the dredging and construction works can have, and in line with
the Order of the Ministry of the Environment, mitigating measures in this respect should be
taken. The most simple of these is to plan the execution of the works in periods not interfering
with those of spawning or intense migration.
A second mitigating measure is to minimise all causes of resuspension by adjusting dredging
and disposal techniques (limited or no overflow from dredgers and barges).
A third option is to prohibit dredging to be carried out in the whole width of the channel at a time.
However, this is not to be expected in view of ongoing shipping in the port areas.
A fourth option is to simply ban all dredging works in the entire area or specific areas, in order to
protect the breakwater spawning areas during certain periods. It is to be noted that all these
measured should be based on accurate and permanent biological monitoring in the area.
Secondly and equally important, all mitigating measures should be clearly stated in the dredging
contract.
DISPOSAL AREA
As part of the project more than 700.000 m3 of glacial till and silty sediments will be dumped at
the offshore disposal site No. 3, which is located approximately 20 kilometres south-west from
the port gates. This site is in operation as a disposal site for dredged sediments since 1987 and
at present more than 10 million m3 of dredged sediments have been dumped here. This includes
both clean and contaminated sediments.
An importanit consequence of the physical presence of dredged sediment disposal activities is
interference with fishery activities. This relates to both smothering of areas potentially used for
fisheries and bottom topography changes which causes these areas to become unsuitable for
certain types of fishing gear (bottom trawling).
Investigations carried out in 1998 by a mixed team of experts from i.a. the Fishery Research
Laboratory and the Klaipeda University have demonstrated that the disposal activities carried out
during the past ten years have been far from accurate. Most of the dumpings have taken place
outside the planned disposal area, probably as a consequence of inaccurate positioning or to
save money on fuel consumption by dumping at closer range to the port (see figure 6-3). In
total, the area where dredged sediments have been dumped is more than 9 times the surface
area of the official disposal site. This being a prime area for cod fishing, it will be clear that
considerable damage has been caused to the cod fishing industry, in which approximately 250
fishermen are active. Approximately 200 km2 of the sea bottom has become unsuitable for
bottom trawling fisheries.
In addition, one of the effects of dumping contaminated sediments at the offshore disposal sites
has also been recorded, as several flatfish species caught in the area (i.e. river flounder and
turbot) have skin diseases.
To minimise the negative effects of dumping of dredged sediments offshore, the following
aspects should be taken into account:
H7371   Draft Final Environmental Impact Assessment   6-10                     September 1999



Klaipeda Port Entrance Rehabilitation Project                                             t
HARRIS
*  Disposal should be restricted to the official boundaries of the No. 3 disposal site, as this area
has already been damaged considerably. In addition the bottom sediments present in this
area already consist for a large part of glacial till.
*  No new offshore disposal site should be initiated for disposal of dredged sediments, as this
will only increase the area unsuitable for fisheries. In addition, the plan for a new disposal
site at depths of 50 - 70 meters is not recommendable, as this depth is important from a cod
spawning point of view.
*  Contaminated sediments (above a certain level of contamination) should not be dumped at
sea, but should be stored in an environmentally sound way in a CDF (confined disposal
facility).
